     <p>
      <b>
       Heavy metal
      </b>
      is a genre of
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">
       rock music
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      that developed in the late 1960s and early 1970s, largely in the United States and the United Kingdom.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With roots in
      <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">
       blues rock
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">
       psychedelic rock
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fast_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-fast-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the bands that created heavy metal developed a thick, massive sound, characterized by highly amplified
      <a href="/wiki/Distortion_(music)" title="Distortion (music)">
       distortion
      </a>
      , extended
      <a href="/wiki/Guitar_solo" title="Guitar solo">
       guitar solos
      </a>
      , emphatic beats, and overall loudness. Heavy metal lyrics and performance styles are often associated with
      <a href="/wiki/Masculinity" title="Masculinity">
       masculinity
      </a>
      , aggression, and
      <a href="/wiki/Machismo" title="Machismo">
       machismo
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fast_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-fast-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The first heavy metal bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
       Led Zeppelin
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">
       Black Sabbath
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">
       Deep Purple
      </a>
      attracted large audiences, though they were often derided by critics, a status common throughout the history of the genre. During the mid-1970s,
      <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">
       Judas Priest
      </a>
      helped spur the genre's evolution by discarding much of its
      <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">
       blues
      </a>
      influence;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser_1993.2C_p._6_4-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Walser_1993.2C_p._6-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Motrhead">
       Motrhead
      </a>
      introduced a
      <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">
       punk rock
      </a>
      sensibility and an increasing emphasis on speed. Beginning in the late 1970s, bands in the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_British_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of British Heavy Metal">
       New Wave of British Heavy Metal
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">
       Iron Maiden
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Saxon_(band)" title="Saxon (band)">
       Saxon
      </a>
      followed in a similar vein. Before the end of the decade, heavy metal fans became known as "
      <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_subculture" title="Heavy metal subculture">
       metalheads
      </a>
      " or "
      <a href="/wiki/Headbanging" title="Headbanging">
       headbangers
      </a>
      ".
     </p>
     <p>
      During the 1980s,
      <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">
       glam metal
      </a>
      became a commercial force with groups such as
      <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6tley_Cr%C3%BCe" title="Mtley Cre">
       Mtley Cre
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Poison_(American_band)" title="Poison (American band)">
       Poison
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Underground_music" title="Underground music">
       Underground scenes
      </a>
      produced an array of more aggressive styles:
      <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">
       thrash metal
      </a>
      broke into the mainstream with bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">
       Metallica
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">
       Megadeth
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">
       Slayer
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">
       Anthrax
      </a>
      , while other
      <a href="/wiki/Extreme_metal" title="Extreme metal">
       extreme subgenres of metal
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">
       death metal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">
       black metal
      </a>
      remain
      <a href="/wiki/Subculture" title="Subculture">
       subcultural
      </a>
      phenomena. Since the mid-1990s popular styles have further expanded the definition of the genre. These include
      <a href="/wiki/Groove_metal" title="Groove metal">
       groove metal
      </a>
      (with bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Pantera" title="Pantera">
       Pantera
      </a>
      ), influenced by extreme metal and
      <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">
       hardcore punk
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">
       nu metal
      </a>
      (with bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">
       Slipknot
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">
       Korn
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Linkin_Park" title="Linkin Park">
       Linkin Park
      </a>
      ), which often incorporates elements of
      <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">
       grunge
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">
       hip hop
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

         <span class="tocnumber">
          1
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          Characteristics
         </span>
        </a>
        <ul>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">
       Characteristics
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Characteristics">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Heavy metal is traditionally characterized by loud distorted guitars, emphatic rhythms, dense bass-and-drum sound, and vigorous vocals. Metal subgenres variously emphasize, alter, or omit one or more of these attributes.
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_Times" title="New York Times">
        New York Times
       </a>
      </i>
      critic
      <a href="/wiki/Jon_Pareles" title="Jon Pareles">
       Jon Pareles
      </a>
      writes, "In the taxonomy of popular music, heavy metal is a major subspecies of hard-rockthe breed with less syncopation, less blues, more showmanship and more brute force."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JP_6-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JP-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The typical band lineup includes a
      <a href="/wiki/Drummer" title="Drummer">
       drummer
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Bass_guitar" title="Bass guitar">
       bassist
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_guitar" title="Rhythm guitar">
       rhythm guitarist
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Lead_guitar" title="Lead guitar">
       lead guitarist
      </a>
      , and a singer, who may or may not be an instrumentalist.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keyboard_instruments" title="Keyboard instruments">
       Keyboard instruments
      </a>
      are sometimes used to enhance the fullness of the sound.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W25_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-W25-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/220px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/330px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg/440px-Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Judas_Priest_Retribution_2005_Tour.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">
         Judas Priest
        </a>
        , performing in 2005
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Electric_guitar" title="Electric guitar">
       electric guitar
      </a>
      and the sonic power that it projects through amplification has historically been the key element in heavy metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W23_8-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-W23-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The heavy metal guitar sound comes from a combined use of high volumes and heavy
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" title="Distortion (guitar)">
       distortion
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With rhythm guitar parts, the "heavy crunch sound in heavy metal...[is created by]
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palm_muting" title="Palm muting">
       palm muting
      </a>
      " the strings with the right hand and using distortion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Palm muting creates a tighter, more precise sound and it emphasizes the low end.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The lead role of the guitar in heavy metal often collides with the traditional "frontman" or bandleader role of the vocalist, creating a musical tension as the two "contend for dominance" in a spirit of "affectionate rivalry".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W25_7-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-W25-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Heavy metal "demands the subordination of the voice" to the overall sound of the band. Reflecting metal's roots in the 1960s counterculture, an "explicit display of emotion" is required from the vocals as a sign of authenticity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Critic
      <a href="/wiki/Simon_Frith" title="Simon Frith">
       Simon Frith
      </a>
      claims that the metal singer's "tone of voice" is more important than the lyrics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The prominent role of the bass is also key to the metal sound, and the interplay of bass and guitar is a central element. The bass guitar provides the low-end sound crucial to making the music "heavy".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W24_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-W24-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metal basslines vary widely in complexity, from holding down a low
      <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">
       pedal point
      </a>
      as a foundation to doubling complex riffs and
      <a href="/wiki/Lick_(music)" title="Lick (music)">
       licks
      </a>
      along with the lead and/or rhythm guitars. Some bands feature the bass as a lead instrument, an approach popularized by Metallica's
      <a href="/wiki/Cliff_Burton" title="Cliff Burton">
       Cliff Burton
      </a>
      in the early 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The essence of metal drumming is creating a loud, constant beat for the band using the "trifecta of speed, power, and precision".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metal drumming "requires an exceptional amount of endurance", and drummers have to develop "considerable speed, coordination, and dexterity...to play the intricate patterns" used in metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry_17-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berry-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A characteristic metal drumming technique is the
      <a href="/wiki/Cymbal_choke" title="Cymbal choke">
       cymbal choke
      </a>
      , which consists of striking a cymbal and then immediately silencing it by grabbing it with the other hand (or, in some cases, the same striking hand), producing a burst of sound. The metal drum setup is generally much larger than those employed in other forms of rock music.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W24_14-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-W24-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In live performance,
      <a href="/wiki/Loudness" title="Loudness">
       loudness
      </a>
      an "onslaught of sound," in sociologist
      <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">
       Deena Weinstein
      </a>
      's descriptionis considered vital.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W23_8-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-W23-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In his book
      <i>
       Metalheads
      </i>
      , psychologist Jeffrey Arnett refers to heavy metal concerts as "the sensory equivalent of war."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following the lead set by
      <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">
       Jimi Hendrix
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">
       Cream
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">
       The Who
      </a>
      , early heavy metal acts such as
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">
       Blue Cheer
      </a>
      set new benchmarks for volume. As Blue Cheer's
      <a href="/wiki/Dickie_Peterson" title="Dickie Peterson">
       Dick Peterson
      </a>
      put it, "All we knew was we wanted more power."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 1977 review of a Motrhead concert noted how "excessive volume in particular figured into the band's impact."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Weinstein makes the case that in the same way that
      <a href="/wiki/Melody" title="Melody">
       melody
      </a>
      is the main element of
      <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">
       pop
      </a>
      and rhythm is the main focus of
      <a href="/wiki/House_music" title="House music">
       house music
      </a>
      , powerful sound, timbre, and volume are the key elements of metal. She argues that the loudness is designed to "sweep the listener into the sound" and to provide a "shot of youthful vitality."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W23_8-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-W23-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Musical_language">
       Musical language
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Musical language">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Rhythm_and_tempo">
       Rhythm and tempo
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rhythm and tempo">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="613" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png/500px-Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/00/Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png 2x" width="500"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern2.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        An example of a rhythmic pattern used in heavy metal. The upper stave is a
        <a href="/wiki/Palm_mute" title="Palm mute">
         palm-muted
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Rhythm_guitar" title="Rhythm guitar">
         rhythm guitar
        </a>
        part. The lower stave is the drum part.
        <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
         <tr>
          <td style="width:1px">
          </td>
          <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
           <div class="haudio">
            <div style="padding:4px 0">
             <a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" title="File:Heavy rythmic pattern audio.ogg">
              rhythm audio
             </a>
            </div>
            <div>
             <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">
              <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="6.04589569161" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" poster="//en.wikipedia.org/static/1.26wmf13/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" preload="none" style="width:220px">
               <source data-bandwidth="135599" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (136 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
               </source>
               Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.
               <br/>
               You can
               <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/Heavy_rythmic_pattern_audio.ogg">
                download the clip
               </a>
               or
               <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
                download a player
               </a>
               to play the clip in your browser.
              </audio>
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
            </div>
           </div>
           <hr/>
          </td>
         </tr>
        </table>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The rhythm in metal songs is emphatic, with deliberate stresses. Weinstein observes that the wide array of sonic effects available to metal drummers enables the "rhythmic pattern to take on a complexity within its elemental drive and insistency".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W24_14-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-W24-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In many heavy metal songs, the main groove is characterized by short, two-note or three-note rhythmic figuresgenerally made up of
      <a href="/wiki/Eighth_note" title="Eighth note">
       8th
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Sixteenth_note" title="Sixteenth note">
       16th notes
      </a>
      . These rhythmic figures are usually performed with a
      <a href="/wiki/Staccato" title="Staccato">
       staccato
      </a>
      attack created by using a
      <a href="/wiki/Palm_mute" title="Palm mute">
       palm-muted
      </a>
      technique on the rhythm guitar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Brief, abrupt, and detached
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhythmic_cell" title="Rhythmic cell">
       rhythmic cells
      </a>
      are joined into rhythmic phrases with a distinctive, often jerky texture. These phrases are used to create rhythmic accompaniment and melodic figures called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riff" title="Riff">
       riffs
      </a>
      , which help to establish thematic
      <a href="/wiki/Hook_(music)" title="Hook (music)">
       hooks
      </a>
      . Heavy metal songs also use longer rhythmic figures such as
      <a href="/wiki/Whole_note" title="Whole note">
       whole note
      </a>
      - or dotted quarter note-length chords in slow-tempo
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Power_ballad" title="Power ballad">
       power ballads
      </a>
      . The tempos in early heavy metal music tended to be "slow, even ponderous".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-W24_14-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-W24-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By the late 1970s, however, metal bands were employing a wide variety of tempos. In the 2000s decade, metal tempos range from slow ballad tempos (quarter note = 60
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beats_per_minute" title="Beats per minute">
       beats per minute
      </a>
      ) to extremely fast
      <a href="/wiki/Blast_beat" title="Blast beat">
       blast beat
      </a>
      tempos (quarter note = 350 beats per minute).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Berry-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony">
       Harmony
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Harmony">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      One of the signatures of the genre is the guitar
      <a href="/wiki/Power_chord" title="Power chord">
       power chord
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22">
       <a href="#cite_note-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In technical terms, the power chord is relatively simple: it involves just one main
      <a href="/wiki/Interval_(music)" title="Interval (music)">
       interval
      </a>
      , generally the
      <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fifth" title="Perfect fifth">
       perfect fifth
      </a>
      , though an
      <a href="/wiki/Octave" title="Octave">
       octave
      </a>
      may be added as a doubling of the
      <a href="/wiki/Root_(chord)" title="Root (chord)">
       root
      </a>
      . Although the perfect fifth interval is the most common basis for the power chord,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      power chords are also based on different intervals such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Minor_third" title="Minor third">
       minor third
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Major_third" title="Major third">
       major third
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Perfect_fourth" title="Perfect fourth">
       perfect fourth
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diminished_fifth" title="Diminished fifth">
       diminished fifth
      </a>
      , or
      <a href="/wiki/Minor_sixth" title="Minor sixth">
       minor sixth
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most power chords are also played with a consistent finger arrangement that can be slid easily up and down the
      <a href="/wiki/Fingerboard" title="Fingerboard">
       fretboard
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Typical_harmonic_structures">
       Typical harmonic structures
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Typical harmonic structures">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Heavy metal is usually based on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riff" title="Riff">
       riffs
      </a>
      created with three main harmonic traits: modal scale progressions, tritone and chromatic progressions, and the use of
      <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">
       pedal points
      </a>
      . Traditional heavy metal tends to employ modal scales, in particular the
      <a href="/wiki/Aeolian_mode" title="Aeolian mode">
       Aeolian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Phrygian_mode" title="Phrygian mode">
       Phrygian modes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Harmonically speaking, this means the genre typically incorporates modal chord progressions such as the Aeolian progressions I-VI-VII, I-VII-(VI), or I-VI-IV-VII and Phrygian progressions implying the relation between I and II (I-II-I, I-II-III, or I-II-VII for example). Tense-sounding
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chromatic" title="Chromatic">
       chromatic
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Tritone" title="Tritone">
       tritone
      </a>
      relationships are used in a number of metal chord progressions.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH_28-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MH-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The tritone, an interval spanning three whole tonessuch as C and F#was a forbidden dissonance in medieval ecclesiastical singing, which led monks to call it
      <i>
       diabolus in musica
      </i>
      "the devil in music".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Heavy metal songs often make extensive use of
      <a href="/wiki/Pedal_point" title="Pedal point">
       pedal point
      </a>
      as a harmonic basis. A pedal point is a sustained tone, typically in the bass range, during which at least one foreign (i.e., dissonant) harmony is sounded in the other parts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_classical_music">
       Relationship with classical music
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Relationship with classical music">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="327" data-file-width="323" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg/220px-Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ritchie_Blackmore_1977.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">
         Ritchie Blackmore
        </a>
        , founder of
        <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">
         Deep Purple
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(rock_band)" title="Rainbow (rock band)">
         Rainbow
        </a>
        , known for the neoclassical approach in his guitar performances
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Walser_(musicologist)" title="Robert Walser (musicologist)">
       Robert Walser
      </a>
      argues that, alongside blues and R&amp;B, the "assemblage of disparate musical styles known...as '
      <a href="/wiki/Classical_music" title="Classical music">
       classical music
      </a>
      '" has been a major influence on heavy metal since the genre's earliest days. He claims that metal's "most influential musicians have been guitar players who have also studied classical music. Their appropriation and adaptation of classical models sparked the development of a new kind of guitar virtuosity [and] changes in the harmonic and melodic language of heavy metal."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In an article written for
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grove_Music_Online" title="Grove Music Online">
        Grove Music Online
       </a>
      </i>
      , Walser states that the "1980s brought on ...the widespread adaptation of chord progressions and virtuosic practices from 18th-century European models, especially
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J.S._Bach" title="J.S. Bach">
       Bach
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Vivaldi" title="Antonio Vivaldi">
       Antonio Vivaldi
      </a>
      , by influential guitarists such as
      <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">
       Ritchie Blackmore
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Marty_Friedman" title="Marty Friedman">
       Marty Friedman
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jason_Becker" title="Jason Becker">
       Jason Becker
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Uli_Jon_Roth" title="Uli Jon Roth">
       Uli Jon Roth
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Van_Halen" title="Eddie Van Halen">
       Eddie Van Halen
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Randy_Rhoads" title="Randy Rhoads">
       Randy Rhoads
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Yngwie_Malmsteen" title="Yngwie Malmsteen">
       Yngwie Malmsteen
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kurt Bachmann of
      <a href="/wiki/Believer_(band)" title="Believer (band)">
       Believer
      </a>
      has stated that "If done correctly, metal and classical fit quite well together. Classical and metal are probably the two genres that have the most in common when it comes to feel, texture, creativity."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Although a number of metal musicians cite classical composers as inspiration, classical and metal are rooted in different cultural traditions and practicesclassical in the
      <a href="/wiki/Art_music" title="Art music">
       art music
      </a>
      tradition, metal in the
      <a href="/wiki/Popular_music" title="Popular music">
       popular music
      </a>
      tradition. As
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Musicologists" title="Musicologists">
       musicologists
      </a>
      Nicolas Cook and Nicola Dibben note, "Analyses of popular music also sometimes reveal the influence of 'art traditions.' An example is Walser's linkage of heavy metal music with the ideologies and even some of the performance practices of nineteenth-century
      <a href="/wiki/Romanticism" title="Romanticism">
       Romanticism
      </a>
      . However, it would be clearly wrong to claim that traditions such as blues, rock, heavy metal, rap or dance music derive primarily from 'art music.'"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Lyrical_themes">
       Lyrical themes
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lyrical themes">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      According to scholars David Hatch and Stephen Millward, Black Sabbath, and the numerous metal bands that they inspired, have concentrated lyrically "on dark and depressing subject matter to an extent hitherto unprecedented in any form of pop music". They take as an example Sabbath's second album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(album)" title="Paranoid (album)">
        Paranoid
       </a>
      </i>
      (1970), which "included songs dealing with personal trauma'
      <a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Paranoid (Black Sabbath song)">
       Paranoid
      </a>
      ' and '
      <a href="/wiki/Fairies_Wear_Boots" title="Fairies Wear Boots">
       Fairies Wear Boots
      </a>
      ' (which described the unsavoury side effects of drug-taking)as well as those confronting wider issues, such as the self-explanatory '
      <a href="/wiki/War_Pigs" title="War Pigs">
       War Pigs
      </a>
      ' and '
      <a href="/wiki/Hand_of_Doom_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Hand of Doom (Black Sabbath song)">
       Hand of Doom
      </a>
      .'"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deriving from the genre's roots in blues music, sex is another important topica thread running from Led Zeppelin's suggestive lyrics to the more explicit references of glam and nu metal bands.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="534" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/220px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/330px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg/440px-King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:King_Diamond_live_2006_Moscow_02.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/King_Diamond_(band)" title="King Diamond (band)">
         King Diamond
        </a>
        , known for writing conceptual lyrics about horror stories
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The thematic content of heavy metal has long been a target of criticism. According to
      <a href="/wiki/Jon_Pareles" title="Jon Pareles">
       Jon Pareles
      </a>
      , "Heavy metal's main subject matter is simple and virtually universal. With grunts, moans and subliterary lyrics, it celebrates...a party without limits.... [T]he bulk of the music is stylized and formulaic."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JP_6-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JP-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Music critics have often deemed metal lyrics juvenile and banal, and others
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch">
         <span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (January 2015)">
          who?
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      have objected to what they see as advocacy of
      <a href="/wiki/Misogyny" title="Misogyny">
       misogyny
      </a>
      and the occult. During the 1980s, the
      <a href="/wiki/Parents_Music_Resource_Center" title="Parents Music Resource Center">
       Parents Music Resource Center
      </a>
      petitioned the U.S. Congress to regulate the popular music industry due to what the group asserted were objectionable lyrics, particularly those in heavy metal songs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See.2C_e_2006_pp._104_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-See.2C_e_2006_pp._104-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Music critic
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">
       Robert Christgau
      </a>
      called metal "an expressive mode it sometimes seems will be with us for as long as ordinary whiteboys fear girls, pity themselves, and are permitted to rage against a world they'll never beat".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1990, Judas Priest was sued in American court by the parents of two young men who had shot themselves five years earlier, allegedly after hearing the subliminal statement "do it" in a Priest song. While the case attracted a great deal of media attention, it was ultimately dismissed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-See.2C_e_2006_pp._104_37-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-See.2C_e_2006_pp._104-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In some predominantly Muslim countries, heavy metal has been officially denounced as a threat to traditional values. In countries such as Morocco, Egypt, Lebanon, and Malaysia, there have been incidents of heavy metal musicians and fans being arrested and incarcerated.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Image_and_fashion">
       Image and fashion
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Image and fashion">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_fashion" title="Heavy metal fashion">
       Heavy metal fashion
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="1280" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/220px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/330px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg/440px-KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KISS_in_concert_Boston_2004.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">
         Kiss
        </a>
        performing in 2004, wearing makeup
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      For certain artists and bands, visual imagery plays a large role in heavy metal. In addition to its sound and lyrics, a heavy metal band's "image" is expressed in album sleeve art, logos, stage sets, clothing, and
      <a href="/wiki/Music_video" title="Music video">
       music videos
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Down-the-back long hair, according to Weinstein, is the "most crucial distinguishing feature of metal fashion."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Originally adopted from the hippie subculture, by the 1980s and 1990s heavy metal hair "symbolised the hate, angst and disenchantment of a generation that seemingly never felt at home", according to journalist Nader Rahman. Long hair gave members of the metal community "the power they needed to rebel against nothing in general."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The classic uniform of heavy metal fans consists of light colored, ripped frayed or torn blue jeans, black T-shirts, boots and black leather or jeans jackets.... T-shirts are generally emblazoned with the logos or other visual representations of favorite metal bands."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metal fans also "appropriated elements from the S&amp;M community (chains, metal studs, skulls, leather and crosses)." In the 1980s, a range of sources, from punk and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goth" title="Goth">
       goth
      </a>
      music to horror films, influenced metal fashion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Umelec_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Umelec-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many metal performers of the 1970s and 1980s used radically shaped and brightly colored instruments to enhance their stage appearance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_.282007.29.2C_p._135_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Thompson_.282007.29.2C_p._135-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Fashion and personal style was especially important for glam metal bands of the era. Performers typically wore long, dyed, hairspray-teased hair (hence the nickname, "hair metal"); makeup such as lipstick and eyeliner; gaudy clothing, including leopard-skin-printed shirts or vests and tight denim, leather, or spandex pants; and accessories such as headbands and jewelry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thompson_.282007.29.2C_p._135_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Thompson_.282007.29.2C_p._135-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pioneered by the heavy metal act
      <a href="/wiki/X_Japan" title="X Japan">
       X Japan
      </a>
      in the late 1980s, bands in the Japanese movement known as
      <a href="/wiki/Visual_kei" title="Visual kei">
       visual kei
      </a>
      which includes many nonmetal groupsemphasize elaborate costumes, hair, and makeup.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_gestures">
       Physical gestures
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Physical gestures">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="792" data-file-width="907" height="192" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/220px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/330px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg/440px-Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metsat%C3%B6ll_at_Tuska_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Fans raise their fists and make the "devil horns" gesture at a concert by Estonian heavy metal group
        <a href="/wiki/Metsat%C3%B6ll" title="Metsatll">
         Metsatll
        </a>
        in 2006
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Many metal musicians when performing live engage in
      <a href="/wiki/Headbanging" title="Headbanging">
       headbanging
      </a>
      , which involves rhythmically beating time with the head, often emphasized by long hair. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corna" title="Corna">
       il cornuto
      </a>
      , or devil horns, hand gesture, also widespread, was popularized by vocalist
      <a href="/wiki/Ronnie_James_Dio" title="Ronnie James Dio">
       Ronnie James Dio
      </a>
      while with Black Sabbath and
      <a href="/wiki/Dio_(band)" title="Dio (band)">
       Dio
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MH_28-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MH-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although
      <a href="/wiki/Gene_Simmons" title="Gene Simmons">
       Gene Simmons
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">
       Kiss
      </a>
      claims to have been the first to make the gesture on the 1977
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Love_Gun" title="Love Gun">
        Love Gun
       </a>
      </i>
      album cover, there is speculation as to who started the phenomenon.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Attendees of metal concerts do not dance in the usual sense. Deena Weinstein has argued that this is due to the music's largely male audience and "extreme heterosexualist ideology." She identifies two primary body movements that substitute for dancing: headbanging and an arm thrust that is both a sign of appreciation and a rhythmic gesture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The performance of
      <a href="/wiki/Air_guitar" title="Air guitar">
       air guitar
      </a>
      is popular among metal fans both at concerts and listening to records at home.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fan_subculture">
       Fan subculture
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Fan subculture">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_subculture" title="Heavy metal subculture">
       Heavy metal subculture
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Deena_Weinstein" title="Deena Weinstein">
       Deena Weinstein
      </a>
      argues that heavy metal has outlasted many other rock genres largely due to the emergence of an intense, exclusionary, strongly masculine subculture.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While the metal fanbase is largely young, white, male, and blue-collar, the group is "tolerant of those outside its core demographic base who follow its codes of dress, appearance, and behavior".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Identification with the subculture is strengthened not only by the shared experience of concert-going and shared elements of fashion, but also by contributing to metal magazines and, more recently, websites.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:HeavyMetalJckt.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="465" data-file-width="494" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/220px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/330px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HeavyMetalJckt.jpg/440px-HeavyMetalJckt.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:HeavyMetalJckt.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A heavy metal fan wearing a denim jacket with band patches and artwork of the heavy metal bands
        <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">
         Metallica
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N' Roses">
         Guns N' Roses
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">
         Iron Maiden
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">
         Slipknot
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Dio_(band)" title="Dio (band)">
         Dio
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
         Led Zeppelin
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The metal scene has been characterized as a "subculture of alienation", with its own code of authenticity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JQS_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-JQS-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This code puts several demands on performers: they must appear both completely devoted to their music and loyal to the subculture that supports it; they must appear uninterested in mainstream appeal and radio hits; and they must never "
      <a href="/wiki/Selling_out" title="Selling out">
       sell out
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the fans themselves, the code promotes "opposition to established authority, and separateness from the rest of society".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Musician and filmmaker
      <a href="/wiki/Rob_Zombie" title="Rob Zombie">
       Rob Zombie
      </a>
      observes, "Most of the kids who come to my shows seem like really imaginative kids with a lot of creative energy they don't know what to do with" and that metal is "outsider music for outsiders. Nobody wants to be the weird kid; you just somehow end up being the weird kid. It's kind of like that, but with metal you have all the weird kids in one place".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sam_Dunn_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sam_Dunn-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Scholars of metal have noted the tendency of fans to classify and reject some performers (and some other fans) as "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Poseur_(music)" title="Poseur (music)">
       poseurs
      </a>
      " "who pretended to be part of the subculture, but who were deemed to lack authenticity and sincerity".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JQS_53-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-JQS-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">
       Etymology
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Etymology">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      The origin of the term "heavy metal" in a musical context is uncertain. The phrase has been used for centuries in chemistry and metallurgy, where the periodic table organizes elements of both
      <a href="/wiki/Light_metal" title="Light metal">
       light
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">
       heavy metals
      </a>
      (e.g., uranium). An early use of the term in modern popular culture was by
      <a href="/wiki/Counterculture" title="Counterculture">
       countercultural
      </a>
      writer
      <a href="/wiki/William_S._Burroughs" title="William S. Burroughs">
       William S. Burroughs
      </a>
      . His 1962 novel
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Soft_Machine" title="The Soft Machine">
        The Soft Machine
       </a>
      </i>
      includes a character known as "Uranian Willy, the Heavy Metal Kid". Burroughs's next novel,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nova_Express" title="Nova Express">
        Nova Express
       </a>
      </i>
      (1964), develops the theme, using
      <i>
       heavy metal
      </i>
      as a metaphor for addictive drugs: "With their diseases and orgasm drugs and their sexless parasite life formsHeavy Metal People of Uranus wrapped in cool blue mist of vaporized bank notesAnd The Insect People of Minraud with metal music".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The phrase was later lifted by
      <a href="/wiki/Sandy_Pearlman" title="Sandy Pearlman">
       Sandy Pearlman
      </a>
      , who used the term to describe
      <a href="/wiki/The_Byrds" title="The Byrds">
       The Byrds
      </a>
      for their supposed "aluminium style of context and effect", particularly on their album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Notorious_Byrd_Brothers" title="The Notorious Byrd Brothers">
        The Notorious Byrd Brothers
       </a>
      </i>
      (1968).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dome_59-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dome-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="531" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg/220px-Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg/330px-Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg/440px-Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jimi_Hendrix_1967_uncropped.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Jimi Hendrix, playing in Sweden in 1967
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Metal historian
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_Christe" title="Ian Christe">
       Ian Christe
      </a>
      describes what the components of the term mean in "hippiespeak": "heavy" is roughly synonymous with "potent" or "profound," and "metal" designates a certain type of mood, grinding and weighted as with metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The word "heavy" in this sense was a basic element of
      <a href="/wiki/Beatnik" title="Beatnik">
       beatnik
      </a>
      and later countercultural
      <a href="/wiki/Slang" title="Slang">
       slang
      </a>
      , and references to "heavy music"typically slower, more amplified variations of standard pop farewere already common by the mid-1960s.
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">
       Iron Butterfly
      </a>
      's debut album, released in early 1968, was titled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Heavy_(Iron_Butterfly_album)" title="Heavy (Iron Butterfly album)">
        Heavy
       </a>
      </i>
      . The first use of "heavy metal" in a song lyric is in reference to a motorcycle in the
      <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">
       Steppenwolf
      </a>
      song "
      <a href="/wiki/Born_to_Be_Wild" title="Born to Be Wild">
       Born to Be Wild
      </a>
      ", also released that year:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      "I like smoke and lightning/Heavy metal thunder/Racin' with the wind/And the feelin' that I'm under."
     </p>
     <p>
      The first documented use of the phrase to describe a type of rock music identified to date appears in a review by
      <a href="/wiki/Barry_Gifford" title="Barry Gifford">
       Barry Gifford
      </a>
      . In the May 11, 1968, issue of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
        Rolling Stone
       </a>
      </i>
      , he wrote about the album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/A_Long_Time_Comin%27" title="A Long Time Comin'">
        A Long Time Comin'
       </a>
      </i>
      by U.S. band
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electric_Flag" title="Electric Flag">
       Electric Flag
      </a>
      : "Nobody who's been listening to
      <a href="/wiki/Mike_Bloomfield" title="Mike Bloomfield">
       Mike Bloomfield
      </a>
      either talking or playingin the last few years could have expected this. This is the new soul music, the synthesis of white blues and heavy metal rock."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In January 1970
      <a href="/wiki/Lucian_Truscott#Family" title="Lucian Truscott">
       Lucian K. Truscott IV
      </a>
      reviewing
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_II" title="Led Zeppelin II">
        Led Zeppelin II
       </a>
      </i>
      for the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Village_Voice" title="Village Voice">
       Village Voice
      </a>
      described the sound as "heavy" and made comparisons with
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">
       Blue Cheer
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge" title="Vanilla Fudge">
       Vanilla Fudge
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Other early documented uses of the phrase are from reviews by critic
      <a href="/wiki/Mike_Saunders" title="Mike Saunders">
       Mike Saunders
      </a>
      . In the November 12, 1970 issue of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
        Rolling Stone
       </a>
      </i>
      , he commented on an album put out the previous year by the British band
      <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(band)" title="Humble Pie (band)">
       Humble Pie
      </a>
      : "
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/As_Safe_as_Yesterday_Is" title="As Safe as Yesterday Is">
        Safe as Yesterday Is
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      their first American release, proved that Humble Pie could be boring in lots of different ways. Here they were a noisy, unmelodic, heavy metal-leaden shit-rock band with the loud and noisy parts beyond doubt. There were a couple of nice songs...and one monumental pile of refuse". He described the band's latest,
      <a href="/wiki/Humble_Pie_(album)" title="Humble Pie (album)">
       self-titled release
      </a>
      as "more of the same 27th-rate heavy metal crap".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a review of
      <a href="/wiki/Sir_Lord_Baltimore" title="Sir Lord Baltimore">
       Sir Lord Baltimore
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_Come_(Sir_Lord_Baltimore_album)" title="Kingdom Come (Sir Lord Baltimore album)">
        Kingdom Come
       </a>
      </i>
      in the May 1971
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Creem" title="Creem">
        Creem
       </a>
      </i>
      , Saunders wrote, "Sir Lord Baltimore seems to have down pat most all the best heavy metal tricks in the book".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       Creem
      </i>
      critic
      <a href="/wiki/Lester_Bangs" title="Lester Bangs">
       Lester Bangs
      </a>
      is credited with popularizing the term via his early 1970s essays on bands such as Led Zeppelin and Black Sabbath.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Through the decade,
      <i>
       heavy metal
      </i>
      was used by certain critics as a virtually automatic putdown. In 1979, lead
      <i>
       New York Times
      </i>
      popular music critic
      <a href="/wiki/John_Rockwell" title="John Rockwell">
       John Rockwell
      </a>
      described what he called "heavy-metal rock" as "brutally aggressive music played mostly for minds clouded by drugs",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and, in a different article, as "a crude exaggeration of rock basics that appeals to white teenagers".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Coined by
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">
       Black Sabbath
      </a>
      drummer,
      <a href="/wiki/Bill_Ward_(musician)" title="Bill Ward (musician)">
       Bill Ward
      </a>
      , "downer rock" was one of the earliest terms used to describe this style of music and was applied to acts such as Sabbath and
      <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock" title="Bloodrock">
       Bloodrock
      </a>
      .
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Classic_Rock_(magazine)" title="Classic Rock (magazine)">
        Classic Rock
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine described the downer rock culture revolving around the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaaludes" title="Quaaludes">
       Quaaludes
      </a>
      and the drinking of wine.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Later the term would be replaced by "heavy metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The terms "heavy metal" and "
      <a href="/wiki/Hard_rock" title="Hard rock">
       hard rock
      </a>
      " have often been used interchangeably, particularly in discussing bands of the 1970s, a period when the terms were largely synonymous.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For example, the 1983
      <i>
       Rolling Stone Encyclopedia of Rock &amp; Roll
      </i>
      includes this passage: "known for its aggressive blues-based hard-rock style,
      <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith" title="Aerosmith">
       Aerosmith
      </a>
      was the top American heavy-metal band of the mid-Seventies".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Antecedents:_1950s_to_mid-1960s">
       Antecedents: 1950s to mid-1960s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Antecedents: 1950s to mid-1960s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Heavy metal's quintessential guitar style, built around distortion-heavy riffs and power chords, traces its roots to early 1950s
      <a href="/wiki/Memphis_blues" title="Memphis blues">
       Memphis blues
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Guitarist" title="Guitarist">
       guitarists
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Joe_Hill_Louis" title="Joe Hill Louis">
       Joe Hill Louis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Willie_Johnson_(guitarist)" title="Willie Johnson (guitarist)">
       Willie Johnson
      </a>
      , and particularly
      <a href="/wiki/Pat_Hare" title="Pat Hare">
       Pat Hare
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rolling_memphis_73-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rolling_memphis-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_74-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-palmer-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who captured a "grittier, nastier, more ferocious electric guitar sound" on records such as
      <a href="/wiki/James_Cotton" title="James Cotton">
       James Cotton
      </a>
      's "
      <a href="/wiki/File:James_Cotton_-_Cotton_Crop_Blues.ogg" title="File:James Cotton - Cotton Crop Blues.ogg">
       Cotton Crop Blues
      </a>
      " (1954);
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-palmer_74-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-palmer-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the late 1950s instrumentals of
      <a href="/wiki/Link_Wray" title="Link Wray">
       Link Wray
      </a>
      , particularly "
      <a href="/wiki/Rumble_(instrumental)" title="Rumble (instrumental)">
       Rumble
      </a>
      " (1958);
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the early 1960s
      <a href="/wiki/Surf_music" title="Surf music">
       surf rock
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Dick_Dale" title="Dick Dale">
       Dick Dale
      </a>
      , including "
      <a href="/wiki/Let%27s_Go_Trippin%27" title="Let's Go Trippin'">
       Let's Go Trippin'
      </a>
      " (1961) and "
      <a href="/wiki/Misirlou" title="Misirlou">
       Misirlou
      </a>
      " (1962) and
      <a href="/wiki/The_Kingsmen" title="The Kingsmen">
       The Kingsmen
      </a>
      's version of "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Louie,_Louie" title="Louie, Louie">
       Louie, Louie
      </a>
      " (1963) which made it a
      <a href="/wiki/Garage_rock" title="Garage rock">
       garage rock
      </a>
      standard.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="498" data-file-width="500" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/220px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/330px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png/440px-Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cream_on_Fanclub_1968.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Cream performing on the Dutch television program Fanclub in 1968
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      However, the genre's direct lineage begins in the mid-1960s. American blues music was a major influence on the early
      <a href="/wiki/British_rock" title="British rock">
       British rockers
      </a>
      of the era. Bands like
      <a href="/wiki/The_Rolling_Stones" title="The Rolling Stones">
       The Rolling Stones
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/The_Yardbirds" title="The Yardbirds">
       The Yardbirds
      </a>
      developed
      <a href="/wiki/Blues_rock" title="Blues rock">
       blues rock
      </a>
      by recording covers of many classic blues songs, often speeding up the
      <a href="/wiki/Tempo" title="Tempo">
       tempos
      </a>
      . As they experimented with the music, the UK blues-based bandsand the U.S. acts they influenced in turndeveloped what would become the hallmarks of heavy metal, in particular, the loud, distorted guitar sound.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_19-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Kinks" title="The Kinks">
       The Kinks
      </a>
      played a major role in popularising this sound with their 1964 hit "
      <a href="/wiki/You_Really_Got_Me" title="You Really Got Me">
       You Really Got Me
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In addition to The Kinks'
      <a href="/wiki/Dave_Davies" title="Dave Davies">
       Dave Davies
      </a>
      , other guitarists such as
      <a href="/wiki/The_Who" title="The Who">
       The Who
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Pete_Townshend" title="Pete Townshend">
