       Pete Townshend
      </a>
      and The Yardbirds'
      <a href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck" title="Jeff Beck">
       Jeff Beck
      </a>
      were experimenting with feedback.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Where the blues rock drumming style started out largely as simple shuffle beats on small kits, drummers began using a more muscular, complex, and amplified approach to match and be heard against the increasingly loud guitar.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyaqji_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Vocalists similarly modified their technique and increased their reliance on amplification, often becoming more stylized and dramatic. In terms of sheer volume, especially in live performance, The Who's "bigger-louder-wall-of-
      <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Amplification#Marshall_Stack" title="Marshall Amplification">
       Marshalls
      </a>
      " approach was seminal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The combination of blues rock with
      <a href="/wiki/Psychedelic_rock" title="Psychedelic rock">
       psychedelic rock
      </a>
      formed much of the original basis for heavy metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One of the most influential bands in forging the merger of genres was the British power trio
      <a href="/wiki/Cream_(band)" title="Cream (band)">
       Cream
      </a>
      , who derived a massive, heavy sound from
      <a href="/wiki/Unison" title="Unison">
       unison
      </a>
      riffing between guitarist
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Clapton" title="Eric Clapton">
       Eric Clapton
      </a>
      and bassist
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Bruce" title="Jack Bruce">
       Jack Bruce
      </a>
      , as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Ginger_Baker" title="Ginger Baker">
       Ginger Baker
      </a>
      's double bass drumming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Their first two LPs,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Fresh_Cream" title="Fresh Cream">
        Fresh Cream
       </a>
      </i>
      (1966) and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Disraeli_Gears" title="Disraeli Gears">
        Disraeli Gears
       </a>
      </i>
      (1967), are regarded as essential prototypes for the future style.
      <a href="/wiki/The_Jimi_Hendrix_Experience" title="The Jimi Hendrix Experience">
       The Jimi Hendrix Experience
      </a>
      's debut album,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Are_You_Experienced_(album)" title="Are You Experienced (album)">
        Are You Experienced
       </a>
      </i>
      (1967), was also highly influential.
      <a href="/wiki/Jimi_Hendrix" title="Jimi Hendrix">
       Hendrix
      </a>
      's virtuosic technique would be emulated by many metal guitarists and the album's most successful single, "
      <a href="/wiki/Purple_Haze" title="Purple Haze">
       Purple Haze
      </a>
      ", is identified by some as the first heavy metal hit.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-vdqxbw_19-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-vdqxbw-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      During the late sixties, many psychedelic singers such as
      <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Brown_(musician)" title="Arthur Brown (musician)">
       Arthur Brown
      </a>
      , began to create outlandish, theatrical and often macabre performances; which in itself became incredibly influential to many metal acts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge" title="Vanilla Fudge">
       Vanilla Fudge
      </a>
      , whose
      <a href="/wiki/Vanilla_Fudge_(album)" title="Vanilla Fudge (album)">
       first album
      </a>
      also came out in 1967, has been called "one of the few American links between psychedelia and what soon became heavy metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Origins:_late_1960s_and_early_1970s">
       Origins: late 1960s and early 1970s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Origins: late 1960s and early 1970s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Traditional_heavy_metal" title="Traditional heavy metal">
       Traditional heavy metal
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_(1970%27s).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="660" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/170px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/255px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg/340px-John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_%281970%27s%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:John_Kay_of_Steppenwolf_(1970%27s).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        John Kay of
        <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">
         Steppenwolf
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Critics disagree over who can be thought of as the first heavy metal band. Most credit either
      <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
       Led Zeppelin
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">
       Black Sabbath
      </a>
      , with American commentators tending to favour Led Zeppelin and British commentators tending to favour Black Sabbath, though many give equal credit to both. A few commentatorsmainly Americanargue for other groups including
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Butterfly" title="Iron Butterfly">
       Iron Butterfly
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">
       Steppenwolf
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">
       Blue Cheer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1968, the sound that would become known as heavy metal began to coalesce. That January, the San Francisco band
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_Cheer" title="Blue Cheer">
       Blue Cheer
      </a>
      released a cover of
      <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cochran" title="Eddie Cochran">
       Eddie Cochran
      </a>
      's classic "
      <a href="/wiki/Summertime_Blues" title="Summertime Blues">
       Summertime Blues
      </a>
      ", from their debut album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Vincebus_Eruptum" title="Vincebus Eruptum">
        Vincebus Eruptum
       </a>
      </i>
      , that many consider the first true heavy metal recording.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        89
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The same month,
      <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(band)" title="Steppenwolf (band)">
       Steppenwolf
      </a>
      released its
      <a href="/wiki/Steppenwolf_(Steppenwolf_album)" title="Steppenwolf (Steppenwolf album)">
       self-titled debut album
      </a>
      , including "
      <a href="/wiki/Born_to_Be_Wild" title="Born to Be Wild">
       Born to Be Wild
      </a>
      ", which refers to "heavy metal thunder" in describing a motorcycle. In July, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jeff_Beck_Group" title="Jeff Beck Group">
       Jeff Beck Group
      </a>
      , whose leader had preceded Page as The Yardbirds' guitarist, released its debut record:
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Truth_(Jeff_Beck_album)" title="Truth (Jeff Beck album)">
        Truth
       </a>
      </i>
      featured some of the "most molten, barbed, downright funny noises of all time," breaking ground for generations of metal ax-slingers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90">
       <a href="#cite_note-90">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        90
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In October, Page's new band,
      <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
       Led Zeppelin
      </a>
      , made its live debut.
      <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles" title="The Beatles">
       The Beatles
      </a>
      '
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Beatles_(album)" title="The Beatles (album)">
        White Album
       </a>
      </i>
      , which also came out that month, included "
      <a href="/wiki/Helter_Skelter_(song)" title="Helter Skelter (song)">
       Helter Skelter
      </a>
      ", then one of the heaviest-sounding songs ever released by a major band.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91">
       <a href="#cite_note-91">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        91
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Pretty_Things" title="The Pretty Things">
       The Pretty Things
      </a>
      '
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_opera" title="Rock opera">
       rock opera
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/S.F._Sorrow" title="S.F. Sorrow">
        S.F. Sorrow
       </a>
      </i>
      , released in December, featured "proto heavy metal" songs such as "Old Man Going" and "I See You".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
       <a href="#cite_note-92">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        92
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
       <a href="#cite_note-93">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        93
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In this
      <a href="/wiki/Counterculture_of_the_1960s" title="Counterculture of the 1960s">
       counterculture
      </a>
      period
      <a href="/wiki/MC5" title="MC5">
       MC5
      </a>
      , who began as part of the Detroit garage rock scene, developed a raw distorted style that has been seen as a major influence on the future sound of both heavy metal and later punk music.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
       <a href="#cite_note-94">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        94
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95">
       <a href="#cite_note-95">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        95
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/The_Stooges" title="The Stooges">
       The Stooges
      </a>
      also began to establish and influence a heavy metal and later punk sound, with songs such as "
      <a href="/wiki/I_Wanna_Be_Your_Dog" title="I Wanna Be Your Dog">
       I Wanna Be Your Dog
      </a>
      ", featuring pounding and distorted heavy guitar power chord riffs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
       <a href="#cite_note-96">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        96
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Pink_Floyd" title="Pink Floyd">
       Pink Floyd
      </a>
      released two of their heaviest and loudest songs to date; "
      <a href="/wiki/Ibiza_Bar" title="Ibiza Bar">
       Ibiza Bar
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/The_Nile_Song" title="The Nile Song">
       The Nile Song
      </a>
      ", which was regarded as "one of the heaviest songs the band recorded".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
       <a href="#cite_note-97">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        97
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
       <a href="#cite_note-98">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        98
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/King_Crimson" title="King Crimson">
       King Crimson
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/In_the_Court_of_the_Crimson_King" title="In the Court of the Crimson King">
       debut album
      </a>
      included
      <a href="/wiki/21st_Century_Schizoid_Man" title="21st Century Schizoid Man">
       21st Century Schizoid Man
      </a>
      , which was considered heavy metal by several critics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
       <a href="#cite_note-99">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        99
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
       <a href="#cite_note-100">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        100
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg">
        <img alt="A colour photograph of the four members of Led Zeppelin performing onstage, with some other figures visible in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/220px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/330px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg/440px-LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LedZeppelinChicago75_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
         Led Zeppelin
        </a>
        performing at
        <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Stadium" title="Chicago Stadium">
         Chicago Stadium
        </a>
        in January 1975
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In January 1969, Led Zeppelin's
      <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_(album)" title="Led Zeppelin (album)">
       self-titled debut album
      </a>
      was released and reached number 10 on the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">
        Billboard
       </a>
      </i>
      album chart. In July, Zeppelin and a power trio with a Cream-inspired, but cruder sound,
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Funk_Railroad" title="Grand Funk Railroad">
       Grand Funk Railroad
      </a>
      , played the
      <a href="/wiki/Atlanta_International_Pop_Festival_(1969)" title="Atlanta International Pop Festival (1969)">
       Atlanta Pop Festival
      </a>
      . That same month, another Cream-rooted trio led by
      <a href="/wiki/Leslie_West" title="Leslie West">
       Leslie West
      </a>
      released
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mountain_(Leslie_West_album)" title="Mountain (Leslie West album)">
        Mountain
       </a>
      </i>
      , an album filled with heavy blues rock guitar and roaring vocals. In August, the groupnow itself dubbed
      <a href="/wiki/Mountain_(band)" title="Mountain (band)">
       Mountain
      </a>
      played an hour-long set at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Woodstock_Festival" title="Woodstock Festival">
       Woodstock Festival
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101">
       <a href="#cite_note-101">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        101
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Led Zeppelin defined central aspects of the emerging genre, with Page's highly distorted guitar style and singer
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Plant" title="Robert Plant">
       Robert Plant
      </a>
      's dramatic, wailing vocals.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
       <a href="#cite_note-102">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        102
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other bands, with a more consistently heavy, "purely" metal sound, would prove equally important in codifying the genre. The 1970 releases by
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath" title="Black Sabbath">
       Black Sabbath
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Black_Sabbath_(album)" title="Black Sabbath (album)">
        Black Sabbath
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(album)" title="Paranoid (album)">
        Paranoid
       </a>
      </i>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">
       Deep Purple
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_Rock_(Deep_Purple_album)" title="In Rock (Deep Purple album)">
        In Rock
       </a>
      </i>
      ) were crucial in this regard.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyaqji_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Led_Zeppelin_Whole_Lotta_Love.ogg" title="File:Led Zeppelin Whole Lotta Love.ogg">
           "Whole Lotta Love"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">

             download the clip
            </a>

            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">

            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          Sample of "
          <a href="/wiki/Whole_Lotta_Love" title="Whole Lotta Love">
           Whole Lotta Love
          </a>
          " by
          <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin" title="Led Zeppelin">
           Led Zeppelin
          </a>
          , from
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_II" title="Led Zeppelin II">
            Led Zeppelin II
           </a>
          </i>
          (1969). The heavy riff-based song, using lyrics culled from the blues.
          <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103">
           <a href="#cite_note-103">
            <span>
             [
            </span>
            103
            <span>
             ]
            </span>
           </a>
          </sup>
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <span>
         <i>
          Problems playing this file? See
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
           media help
          </a>
          .
         </i>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Black Sabbath had developed a particularly heavy sound in part due to an industrial accident guitarist
      <a href="/wiki/Tony_Iommi" title="Tony Iommi">
       Tony Iommi
      </a>
      suffered before cofounding the band. Unable to play normally, Iommi had to tune his guitar down for easier fretting and rely on power chords with their relatively simple fingering.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
       <a href="#cite_note-104">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        104
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Deep Purple had fluctuated between styles in its early years, but by 1969 vocalist
      <a href="/wiki/Ian_Gillan" title="Ian Gillan">
       Ian Gillan
      </a>
      and guitarist
      <a href="/wiki/Ritchie_Blackmore" title="Ritchie Blackmore">
       Ritchie Blackmore
      </a>
      had led the band toward the developing heavy metal style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
       <a href="#cite_note-105">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        105
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1970, Black Sabbath and Deep Purple scored major UK chart hits with "
      <a href="/wiki/Paranoid_(Black_Sabbath_song)" title="Paranoid (Black Sabbath song)">
       Paranoid
      </a>
      " and "
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Night" title="Black Night">
       Black Night
      </a>
      ", respectively.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RnR_Hall_of_Fame_106-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-RnR_Hall_of_Fame-106">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        106
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107">
       <a href="#cite_note-107">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        107
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      That same year, two other British bands released debut albums in a heavy metal mode:
      <a href="/wiki/Uriah_Heep_(band)" title="Uriah Heep (band)">
       Uriah Heep
      </a>
      with
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Very_%27Eavy..._Very_%27Umble" title="Very 'Eavy... Very 'Umble">
        Very 'Eavy... Very 'Umble
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/UFO_(band)" title="UFO (band)">
       UFO
      </a>
      with
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unidentified_Flying_Object_(album)" title="Unidentified Flying Object (album)">
        UFO 1
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock" title="Bloodrock">
       Bloodrock
      </a>
      released their
      <a href="/wiki/Bloodrock_(album)" title="Bloodrock (album)">
       self-titled debut album
      </a>
      , containing a collection of heavy guitar riffs, gruff style vocals and sadistic and macabre lyrics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108">
       <a href="#cite_note-108">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        108
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Budgie_(band)" title="Budgie (band)">
       Budgie
      </a>
      brought the new metal sound into a power trio context.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109">
       <a href="#cite_note-109">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        109
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The occult lyrics and imagery employed by Black Sabbath and Uriah Heep would prove particularly influential; Led Zeppelin also began foregrounding such elements with its
      <a href="/wiki/Led_Zeppelin_IV" title="Led Zeppelin IV">
       fourth album
      </a>
      , released in 1971.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110">
       <a href="#cite_note-110">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        110
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="683" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/170px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/255px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg/340px-Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phil-Lynott_Thin_Lizzy.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Brian_Robertson" title="Brian Robertson">
         Brian Robertson
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Phil_Lynott" title="Phil Lynott">
         Phil Lynott
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Scott_Gorham" title="Scott Gorham">
         Scott Gorham
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy" title="Thin Lizzy">
         Thin Lizzy
        </a>
        performing during the Bad Reputation Tour, 24 November 1977
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On the other side of the Atlantic, the trend-setting group was
      <a href="/wiki/Grand_Funk_Railroad" title="Grand Funk Railroad">
       Grand Funk Railroad
      </a>
      , described as "the most commercially successful American heavy-metal band from 1970 until they disbanded in 1976, [they] established the Seventies success formula: continuous touring".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111">
       <a href="#cite_note-111">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        111
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other bands identified with metal emerged in the U.S., such as
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_%C3%96yster_Cult" title="Blue yster Cult">
       Blue yster Cult
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_%C3%96yster_Cult_(album)" title="Blue yster Cult (album)">
       1972
      </a>
      ),
      <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith" title="Aerosmith">
       Aerosmith
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Aerosmith_(album)" title="Aerosmith (album)">
       1973
      </a>
      ) and
      <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(band)" title="Kiss (band)">
       Kiss
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Kiss_(Kiss_album)" title="Kiss (Kiss album)">
       1974
      </a>
      ). In Germany,
      <a href="/wiki/Scorpions_(band)" title="Scorpions (band)">
       Scorpions
      </a>
      debuted with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Lonesome_Crow" title="Lonesome Crow">
        Lonesome Crow
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1972. Blackmore, who had emerged as a virtuoso soloist with Deep Purple's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Machine_Head_(album)" title="Machine Head (album)">
        Machine Head
       </a>
      </i>
      (1972), quit the group in 1975 to form
      <a href="/wiki/Rainbow_(rock_band)" title="Rainbow (rock band)">
       Rainbow
      </a>
      . These bands also built audiences via constant touring and increasingly elaborate stage shows.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eyaqji_80-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-eyaqji-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      As described above, there are arguments about whether these and other early bands truly qualify as "heavy metal" or simply as "hard rock". Those closer to the music's blues roots or placing greater emphasis on melody are now commonly ascribed the latter label.
      <a href="/wiki/AC/DC" title="AC/DC">
       AC/DC
      </a>
      , which debuted with
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_Voltage_(Australian_album)" title="High Voltage (Australian album)">
        High Voltage
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1975, is a prime example. The 1983
      <i>
       Rolling Stone
      </i>
      encyclopedia entry begins, "Australian heavy-metal band AC/DC".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112">
       <a href="#cite_note-112">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        112
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Rock historian Clinton Walker writes, "Calling AC/DC a heavy metal band in the seventies was as inaccurate as it is today.... [They] were a rock 'n' roll band that just happened to be heavy enough for metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113">
       <a href="#cite_note-113">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        113
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The issue is not only one of shifting definitions, but also a persistent distinction between musical style and audience identification: Ian Christe describes how the band "became the stepping-stone that led huge numbers of hard rock fans into heavy metal perdition".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114">
       <a href="#cite_note-114">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        114
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In certain cases, there is little debate. After Black Sabbath, the next major example is Britain's
      <a href="/wiki/Judas_Priest" title="Judas Priest">
       Judas Priest
      </a>
      , which debuted with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rocka_Rolla" title="Rocka Rolla">
        Rocka Rolla
       </a>
      </i>
      in 1974. In Christe's description,
     </p>
     <blockquote>
      <p>
       "Black Sabbath's audience was...left to scavenge for sounds with similar impact. By the mid-1970s, heavy metal aesthetic could be spotted, like a mythical beast, in the moody bass and complex dual guitars of
       <a href="/wiki/Thin_Lizzy" title="Thin Lizzy">
        Thin Lizzy
       </a>
       , in the stagecraft of
       <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">
        Alice Cooper
       </a>
       , in the sizzling guitar and showy vocals of
       <a href="/wiki/Queen_(band)" title="Queen (band)">
        Queen
       </a>
       , and in the thundering medieval questions of Rainbow.... Judas Priest arrived to unify and amplify these diverse highlights from hard rock's sonic palette. For the first time, heavy metal became a true genre unto itself."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115">
        <a href="#cite_note-115">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         115
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Though Judas Priest did not have a top 40 album in the United States until 1980, for many it was the definitive post-Sabbath heavy metal band; its twin-guitar attack, featuring rapid tempos and a non-
      <a href="/wiki/Blues" title="Blues">
       blues
      </a>
      y, more cleanly metallic sound, was a major influence on later acts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walser_1993.2C_p._6_4-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Walser_1993.2C_p._6-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While heavy metal was growing in popularity, most critics were not enamored of the music. Objections were raised to metal's adoption of visual spectacle and other trappings of commercial artifice,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnwtey_116-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gnwtey-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but the main offense was its perceived musical and lyrical vacuity: reviewing a Black Sabbath album in the early 1970s, leading critic
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Christgau" title="Robert Christgau">
       Robert Christgau
      </a>
      described it as "dull and decadent...dim-witted, amoral exploitation."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117">
       <a href="#cite_note-117">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        117
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Mainstream:_late_1970s_and_1980s">
       Mainstream: late 1970s and 1980s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mainstream: late 1970s and 1980s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1157" data-file-width="1437" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/220px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/330px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg/440px-Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Maiden_-_bass_and_guitars_30nov2006.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">
         Iron Maiden
        </a>
        , one of the central bands in the
        <a href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_British_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of British Heavy Metal">
         New Wave of British Heavy Metal
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Punk_rock" title="Punk rock">
       Punk rock
      </a>
      emerged in the mid-1970s as a reaction against contemporary social conditions as well as what was perceived as the overindulgent, overproduced rock music of the time, including heavy metal. Sales of heavy metal records declined sharply in the late 1970s in the face of punk,
      <a href="/wiki/Disco" title="Disco">
       disco
      </a>
      , and more mainstream rock.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gnwtey_116-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gnwtey-116">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        116
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With the major labels fixated on punk, many newer British heavy metal bands were inspired by the movement's aggressive, high-energy sound and "
      <a href="/wiki/Low_fidelity" title="Low fidelity">
       lo-fi
      </a>
      ",
      <a href="/wiki/Do_it_yourself" title="Do it yourself">
       do it yourself
      </a>
      ethos. Underground metal bands began putting out cheaply recorded releases independently to small, devoted audiences.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118">
       <a href="#cite_note-118">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        118
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Mot%C3%B6rhead" title="Motrhead">
       Motrhead
      </a>
      , founded in 1975, was the first important band to straddle the punk/metal divide. With the explosion of punk in 1977, others followed. British music papers such as the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/NME" title="NME">
        NME
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sounds_(magazine)" title="Sounds (magazine)">
        Sounds
       </a>
      </i>
      took notice, with
      <i>
       Sounds
      </i>
      writer Geoff Barton christening the movement the "
      <a href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_British_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of British Heavy Metal">
       New Wave of British Heavy Metal
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119">
       <a href="#cite_note-119">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        119
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      NWOBHM bands including
      <a href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden" title="Iron Maiden">
       Iron Maiden
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Saxon_(band)" title="Saxon (band)">
       Saxon
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Def_Leppard" title="Def Leppard">
       Def Leppard
      </a>
      reenergized the heavy metal genre. Following the lead set by Judas Priest and Motrhead, they toughened up the sound, reduced its blues elements, and emphasized increasingly fast tempos.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120">
       <a href="#cite_note-120">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        120
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By 1980, the NWOBHM had broke into the mainstream, as albums by Iron Maiden and Saxon, as well as Motrhead, reached the British top 10. Though less commercially successful, other NWOBHM bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Venom_(band)" title="Venom (band)">
       Venom
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Diamond_Head_(band)" title="Diamond Head (band)">
       Diamond Head
      </a>
      would have a significant influence on metal's development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1981, Motrhead became the first of this new breed of metal bands to top the UK charts with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/No_Sleep_%27til_Hammersmith" title="No Sleep 'til Hammersmith">
        No Sleep 'til Hammersmith
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      The first generation of metal bands was ceding the limelight. Deep Purple had broken up soon after Blackmore's departure in 1975, and Led Zeppelin broke up following drummer
      <a href="/wiki/John_Bonham" title="John Bonham">
       John Bonham
      </a>
      's death in 1980. Black Sabbath plagued with infighting and substance abuse, while facing fierce competition with their opening band, the
      <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">
       Los Angeles
      </a>
      band
      <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen" title="Van Halen">
       Van Halen
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Van_Halen" title="Eddie Van Halen">
       Eddie Van Halen
      </a>
      established himself as one of the leading metal guitarists of the erahis solo on "
      <a href="/wiki/Eruption_(instrumental)" title="Eruption (instrumental)">
       Eruption
      </a>
      ", from the band's
      <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen_(album)" title="Van Halen (album)">
       self-titled 1978 album
      </a>
      , is considered a milestone.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124">
       <a href="#cite_note-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Inspired by Van Halen's success, a metal scene began to develop in Southern California during the late 1970s. Based on the clubs of L.A.'s
      <a href="/wiki/Sunset_Strip" title="Sunset Strip">
       Sunset Strip
      </a>
      , bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Quiet_Riot" title="Quiet Riot">
       Quiet Riot
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ratt" title="Ratt">
       Ratt
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6tley_Cr%C3%BCe" title="Mtley Cre">
       Mtley Cre
      </a>
      , and W.A.S.P. were influenced by traditional heavy metal of the earlier 1970s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These acts incorporated the theatrics (and sometimes makeup) of
      <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">
       glam metal
      </a>
      or "hair metal" such as
      <a href="/wiki/Alice_Cooper" title="Alice Cooper">
       Alice Cooper
      </a>
      and Kiss
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Hair/glam metal bands were often visually distinguished by long, overworked hair styles accompanied by wardrobes which were sometimes considered cross-gender. The lyrics of these
      <a href="/wiki/Glam_metal" title="Glam metal">
       glam metal
      </a>
      bands characteristically emphasized
      <a href="/wiki/Hedonism" title="Hedonism">
       hedonism
      </a>
      and wild behavior, including lyrics which involved sexual expletives and the use of narcotics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_127-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;">
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-image">
        <div class="center">
         <div class="floatnone">
          <img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/50px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/75px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg/100px-Gnome-mime-sound-openclipart.svg.png 2x" width="50"/>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">

          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">

          <a href="/wiki/Hot_for_Teacher" title="Hot for Teacher">
