          </a>
          " by
          <a href="/wiki/Van_Halen" title="Van Halen">
           Van Halen
          </a>
          , from the album
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/1984_(Van_Halen_album)" title="1984 (Van Halen album)">
            1984
           </a>
          </i>
          (1984). This sample demonstrates their sound's similarity to the glam metal style.
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <span>
         <i>
          Problems playing these files? See
          <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">
           media help
          </a>
          .
         </i>
        </span>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      In the wake of the New Wave of British Heavy Metal and Judas Priest's breakthrough
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/British_Steel_(album)" title="British Steel (album)">
        British Steel
       </a>
      </i>
      (1980), heavy metal became increasingly popular in the early 1980s. Many metal artists benefited from the exposure they received on
      <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">
       MTV
      </a>
      , which began airing in 1981sales often soared if a band's videos screened on the channel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Def Leppard's videos for
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pyromania_(album)" title="Pyromania (album)">
        Pyromania
       </a>
      </i>
      (1983) made them superstars in America and Quiet Riot became the first domestic heavy metal band to top the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      chart with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Metal_Health" title="Metal Health">
        Metal Health
       </a>
      </i>
      (1983). One of the seminal events in metal's growing popularity was the 1983
      <a href="/wiki/US_Festival" title="US Festival">
       US Festival
      </a>
      in California, where the "heavy metal day" featuring Ozzy Osbourne, Van Halen, Scorpions, Mtley Cre, Judas Priest, and others drew the largest audiences of the three-day event.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Between 1983 and 1984, heavy metal went from an 8 percent to a 20 percent share of all recordings sold in the U.S.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several major professional magazines devoted to the genre were launched, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Kerrang!" title="Kerrang!">
        Kerrang!
       </a>
      </i>
      (in 1981) and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Metal_Hammer" title="Metal Hammer">
        Metal Hammer
       </a>
      </i>
      (in 1984), as well as a host of fan journals. In 1985,
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      declared, "Metal has broadened its audience base. Metal music is no longer the exclusive domain of male teenagers. The metal audience has become older (college-aged), younger (pre-teen), and more female".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131">
       <a href="#cite_note-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By the mid-1980s, glam metal was a dominant presence on the U.S. charts,
      <a href="/wiki/Music_television" title="Music television">
       music television
      </a>
      , and the arena concert circuit. New bands such as L.A.'s
      <a href="/wiki/Warrant_(American_band)" title="Warrant (American band)">
       Warrant
      </a>
      and acts from the East Coast like
      <a href="/wiki/Poison_(American_band)" title="Poison (American band)">
       Poison
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cinderella_(band)" title="Cinderella (band)">
       Cinderella
      </a>
      became major draws, while Mtley Cre and Ratt remained very popular. Bridging the stylistic gap between hard rock and glam metal,
      <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">
       New Jersey
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Bon_Jovi" title="Bon Jovi">
       Bon Jovi
      </a>
      became enormously successful with its third album,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Slippery_When_Wet" title="Slippery When Wet">
        Slippery When Wet
       </a>
      </i>
      (1986). The similarly styled Swedish band
      <a href="/wiki/Europe_(band)" title="Europe (band)">
       Europe
      </a>
      became international stars with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Final_Countdown_(album)" title="The Final Countdown (album)">
        The Final Countdown
       </a>
      </i>
      (1986). Its
      <a href="/wiki/The_Final_Countdown_(song)" title="The Final Countdown (song)">
       title track
      </a>
      hit number 1 in 25 countries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1987, MTV launched a show,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Headbanger%27s_Ball" title="Headbanger's Ball">
        Headbanger's Ball
       </a>
      </i>
      , devoted exclusively to heavy metal videos. However, the metal audience had begun to factionalize, with those in many underground metal scenes favoring more extreme sounds and disparaging the popular style as "light metal" or "hair metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      One band that reached diverse audiences was
      <a href="/wiki/Guns_N%27_Roses" title="Guns N' Roses">
       Guns N' Roses
      </a>
      . In contrast to their glam metal contemporaries in L.A., they were seen as much more raw and dangerous. With the release of their chart-topping
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Appetite_for_Destruction" title="Appetite for Destruction">
        Appetite for Destruction
       </a>
      </i>
      (1987), they "recharged and almost single-handedly sustained the Sunset Strip sleaze system for several years".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following year,
      <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane's Addiction">
       Jane's Addiction
      </a>
      emerged from the same L.A. hard-rock club scene with its major label debut,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Nothing%27s_Shocking" title="Nothing's Shocking">
        Nothing's Shocking
       </a>
      </i>
      . Reviewing the album,
      <i>
       Rolling Stone
      </i>
      declared, "as much as any band in existence, Jane's Addiction is the true heir to Led Zeppelin".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The group was one of the first to be identified with the "
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">
       alternative metal
      </a>
      " trend that would come to the fore in the next decade. Meanwhile, new bands such as New York's
      <a href="/wiki/Winger_(band)" title="Winger (band)">
       Winger
      </a>
      and New Jersey's
      <a href="/wiki/Skid_Row_(American_band)" title="Skid Row (American band)">
       Skid Row
      </a>
      sustained the popularity of the glam metal style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Covach_136-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Covach-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_metal_genres:_1980s.2C_1990s.2C_and_2000s">
       Other metal genres: 1980s, 1990s, and 2000s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Other metal genres: 1980s, 1990s, and 2000s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Many
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_heavy_metal_genres" title="List of heavy metal genres">
       subgenres of heavy metal
      </a>
      developed outside of the commercial mainstream during the 1980s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Several attempts have been made to map the complex world of underground metal, most notably by the editors of
      <a href="/wiki/AllMusic" title="AllMusic">
       AllMusic
      </a>
      , as well as critic
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rockdetector" title="Rockdetector">
       Garry Sharpe-Young
      </a>
      . Sharpe-Young's multivolume metal encyclopedia separates the underground into five major categories:
      <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">
       thrash metal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">
       death metal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">
       black metal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Power_metal" title="Power metal">
       power metal
      </a>
      , and the related subgenres of
      <a href="/wiki/Doom_metal" title="Doom metal">
       doom
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Gothic_metal" title="Gothic metal">
       gothic metal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Thrash_metal">
       Thrash metal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Thrash metal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">
       thrash metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slayer,_The_Fields_of_Rock,_2007.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="364" data-file-width="760" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/240px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/360px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg/480px-Slayer%2C_The_Fields_of_Rock%2C_2007.jpg 2x" width="240"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slayer,_The_Fields_of_Rock,_2007.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Thrash metal band
        <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">
         Slayer
        </a>
        performing in 2007 in front of a wall of speaker stacks.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Thrash metal emerged in the early 1980s under the influence of
      <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">
       hardcore punk
      </a>
      and the New Wave of British Heavy Metal,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM_139-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GTM-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      particularly songs in the revved-up style known as
      <a href="/wiki/Speed_metal" title="Speed metal">
       speed metal
      </a>
      . The movement began in the United States, with
      <a href="/wiki/Bay_Area_thrash_metal" title="Bay Area thrash metal">
       Bay Area thrash metal
      </a>
      being the leading scene. The sound developed by thrash groups was faster and more aggressive than that of the original metal bands and their glam metal successors.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GTM_139-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-GTM-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Low-register guitar riffs are typically overlaid with
      <a href="/wiki/Shred_guitar" title="Shred guitar">
       shredding
      </a>
      leads. Lyrics often express
      <a href="/wiki/Nihilism" title="Nihilism">
       nihilistic
      </a>
      views or deal with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_issues" title="Social issues">
       social issues
      </a>
      using visceral, gory language. Thrash has been described as a form of "urban blight music" and "a palefaced cousin of rap".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" title="File:Angel of Death clip.ogg">
           "Angel of Death"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">
           <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="27.877346938776" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_4" poster="//en.wikipedia.org/static/1.26wmf13/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" preload="none" style="width:220px">
            <source data-bandwidth="64727" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (65 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/30/Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
            </source>
            Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.
            <br/>
            You can
            <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/30/Angel_of_Death_clip.ogg">
             download the clip
            </a>
            or
            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
             download a player
            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">
           Slayer
          </a>
          's "
          <a href="/wiki/Angel_of_Death_(Slayer_song)" title="Angel of Death (Slayer song)">
           Angel of Death
          </a>
          ", from
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Reign_in_Blood" title="Reign in Blood">
            Reign in Blood
           </a>
          </i>
          (1986), which features the fast, technically complex musicianship typical of thrash metal
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The subgenre was popularized by the "Big Four of Thrash":
      <a href="/wiki/Metallica" title="Metallica">
       Metallica
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Anthrax_(American_band)" title="Anthrax (American band)">
       Anthrax
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Megadeth" title="Megadeth">
       Megadeth
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Slayer" title="Slayer">
       Slayer
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Three German bands,
      <a href="/wiki/Kreator" title="Kreator">
       Kreator
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Sodom_(band)" title="Sodom (band)">
       Sodom
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Destruction_(band)" title="Destruction (band)">
       Destruction
      </a>
      , played a central role in bringing the style to Europe. Others, including San Francisco Bay Area's
      <a href="/wiki/Testament_(band)" title="Testament (band)">
       Testament
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exodus_(band)" title="Exodus (band)">
       Exodus
      </a>
      , New Jersey's
      <a href="/wiki/Overkill_(band)" title="Overkill (band)">
       Overkill
      </a>
      , and Brazil's
      <a href="/wiki/Sepultura" title="Sepultura">
       Sepultura
      </a>
      , also had a significant impact. Although thrash began as an underground movement, and remained largely that for almost a decade, the leading bands of the scene began to reach a wider audience. Metallica brought the sound into the top 40 of the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      album chart in 1986 with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Master_of_Puppets" title="Master of Puppets">
        Master of Puppets
       </a>
      </i>
      , the genre's first platinum record.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Two years later, the band's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/...And_Justice_for_All_(album)" title="...And Justice for All (album)">
        ...And Justice for All
       </a>
      </i>
      hit number 6, while Megadeth and Anthrax also had top 40 records on the American charts.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Though less commercially successful than the rest of the Big Four, Slayer released one of the genre's definitive records:
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Reign_in_Blood" title="Reign in Blood">
        Reign in Blood
       </a>
      </i>
      (1986) was credited for incorporating heavier guitar
      <a href="/wiki/Timbre" title="Timbre">
       timbres
      </a>
      , and for including explicit depictions of death, suffering, violence and occult into thrash metal's lyricism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Slayer attracted a following among
      <a href="/wiki/White_power_skinhead" title="White power skinhead">
       far-right skinheads
      </a>
      , and accusations of promoting violence and
      <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">
       Nazi
      </a>
      themes have dogged the band.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even though Slayer did not receive substantial media exposure, their music played a key role in the development of
      <a href="/wiki/Extreme_metal" title="Extreme metal">
       extreme metal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the early 1990s, thrash achieved breakout success, challenging and redefining the metal mainstream.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metallica's
      <a href="/wiki/Metallica_(album)" title="Metallica (album)">
       self-titled 1991 album
      </a>
      topped the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      chart,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as the band established international following.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Megadeth's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Countdown_to_Extinction" title="Countdown to Extinction">
        Countdown to Extinction
       </a>
      </i>
      (1992) debuted at number two,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150">
       <a href="#cite_note-150">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        150
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Anthrax and Slayer cracked the top 10,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151">
       <a href="#cite_note-151">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        151
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and albums by regional bands such as Testament and Sepultura entered the top 100.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152">
       <a href="#cite_note-152">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        152
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Death_metal">
       Death metal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Death metal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="753" data-file-width="596" height="190" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/150px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/225px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg/300px-Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chuck_Schuldiner.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">
         Death
        </a>
        's
        <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Schuldiner" title="Chuck Schuldiner">
         Chuck Schuldiner
        </a>
        , "widely recognized as the father of death metal"
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153">
         <a href="#cite_note-153">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          153
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">
       death metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      Thrash soon began to evolve and split into more extreme metal genres. "Slayer's music was directly responsible for the rise of death metal," according to MTV News.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154">
       <a href="#cite_note-154">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        154
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The NWOBHM band Venom was also an important progenitor. The death metal movement in both North America and Europe adopted and emphasized the elements of
      <a href="/wiki/Blasphemy" title="Blasphemy">
       blasphemy
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabolism" title="Diabolism">
       diabolism
      </a>
      employed by such acts. Florida's
      <a href="/wiki/Death_(metal_band)" title="Death (metal band)">
       Death
      </a>
      and the Bay Area's
      <a href="/wiki/Possessed_(band)" title="Possessed (band)">
       Possessed
      </a>
      are recognized as seminal bands in the style. Both groups have been credited with inspiring the subgenre's name, the latter via its 1984 demo
      <i>
       Death Metal
      </i>
      and the song "Death Metal", from its 1985 debut album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Seven_Churches_(album)" title="Seven Churches (album)">
        Seven Churches
       </a>
      </i>
      (1985). In the late 1980s and early 1990s, Swedish death metal became notable and melodic forms of death metal were created.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155">
       <a href="#cite_note-155">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        155
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Death metal utilizes the speed and aggression of both thrash and hardcore, fused with lyrics preoccupied with
      <a href="/wiki/Z_movie" title="Z movie">
       Z-grade
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Slasher_film" title="Slasher film">
       slasher movie
      </a>
      violence and
      <a href="/wiki/Satanism" title="Satanism">
       Satanism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOC27_156-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-LOC27-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Death metal vocals are typically bleak, involving guttural "
      <a href="/wiki/Death_growl" title="Death growl">
       death growls
      </a>
      ", high-pitched
      <a href="/wiki/Screaming_(music)" title="Screaming (music)">
       screaming
      </a>
      , the "death rasp",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VS_157-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-VS-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and other uncommon techniques.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genre.E2.80.94Death_Metal.2FBlack_Metal_158-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Genre.E2.80.94Death_Metal.2FBlack_Metal-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Complementing the deep, aggressive vocal style are downtuned, highly
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Distortion_(guitar)" title="Distortion (guitar)">
       distorted
      </a>
      guitars
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOC27_156-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LOC27-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VS_157-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-VS-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and extremely fast percussion, often with rapid
      <a href="/wiki/Bass_drum" title="Bass drum">
       double bass
      </a>
      drumming and "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wall_of_sound" title="Wall of sound">
       wall of sound
      </a>
      "style
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blast_beats" title="Blast beats">
       blast beats
      </a>
      . Frequent tempo and
      <a href="/wiki/Time_signature" title="Time signature">
       time signature
      </a>
      changes and
      <a href="/wiki/Syncopation" title="Syncopation">
       syncopation
      </a>
      are also typical.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159">
       <a href="#cite_note-159">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        159
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" title="File:Obituary Suffocation.ogg">
           "Suffocation"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">
           <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="29.969977324263" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_5" poster="//en.wikipedia.org/static/1.26wmf13/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" preload="none" style="width:220px">
            <source data-bandwidth="98525" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (99 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/90/Obituary_Suffocation.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
            </source>
            Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.
            <br/>
            You can
            <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/9/90/Obituary_Suffocation.ogg">
             download the clip
            </a>
            or
            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
             download a player
            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          "Suffocation" by
          <a href="/wiki/Obituary_(band)" title="Obituary (band)">
           Obituary
          </a>
          from the album
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Slowly_We_Rot" title="Slowly We Rot">
            Slowly We Rot
           </a>
          </i>
          (1989)
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      Death metal, like thrash metal, generally rejects the theatrics of earlier metal styles, opting instead for an everyday look of ripped jeans and plain leather jackets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MS28_160-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-MS28-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      One major exception to this rule was
      <a href="/wiki/Deicide_(band)" title="Deicide (band)">
       Deicide
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Glen_Benton" title="Glen Benton">
       Glen Benton
      </a>
      , who branded an inverted cross on his forehead and wore armor on stage.
      <a href="/wiki/Morbid_Angel" title="Morbid Angel">
       Morbid Angel
      </a>
      adopted
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-fascist" title="Neo-fascist">
       neo-fascist
      </a>
      imagery.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MS28_160-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-MS28-160">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        160
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These two bands, along with Death and
      <a href="/wiki/Obituary_(band)" title="Obituary (band)">
       Obituary
      </a>
      , were leaders of the major death metal scene that emerged in Florida in the mid-1980s. In the UK, the related style of
      <a href="/wiki/Grindcore" title="Grindcore">
       grindcore
      </a>
      , led by bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Napalm_Death" title="Napalm Death">
       Napalm Death
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Extreme_Noise_Terror" title="Extreme Noise Terror">
       Extreme Noise Terror
      </a>
      , emerged from the
      <a href="/wiki/Anarcho-punk" title="Anarcho-punk">
       anarcho-punk
      </a>
      movement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LOC27_156-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-LOC27-156">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        156
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burzum_aske.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/77/Burzum_aske.jpg/200px-Burzum_aske.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/77/Burzum_aske.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/77/Burzum_aske.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burzum_aske.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Photo of the burned ruins of
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fantoft_stave_church" title="Fantoft stave church">
         Fantoft stave church
        </a>
        as depicted on
        <a href="/wiki/Burzum" title="Burzum">
         Burzum
        </a>
        's 1992 EP
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Aske_(EP)" title="Aske (EP)">
          Aske
         </a>
        </i>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Black_metal">
       Black metal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Black metal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Black_metal" title="Black metal">
       black metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      The first wave of black metal emerged in Europe in the early and mid-1980s, led by Britain's
      <a href="/wiki/Venom_(band)" title="Venom (band)">
       Venom
      </a>
      , Denmark's
      <a href="/wiki/Mercyful_Fate" title="Mercyful Fate">
       Mercyful Fate
      </a>
      , Switzerland's
      <a href="/wiki/Hellhammer" title="Hellhammer">
       Hellhammer
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Celtic_Frost" title="Celtic Frost">
       Celtic Frost
      </a>
      , and Sweden's
      <a href="/wiki/Bathory_(band)" title="Bathory (band)">
       Bathory
      </a>
      . By the late 1980s, Norwegian bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">
       Mayhem
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Burzum" title="Burzum">
       Burzum
      </a>
      were heading a second wave.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161">
       <a href="#cite_note-161">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        161
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Black metal varies considerably in style and production quality, although most bands emphasize shrieked and growled vocals, highly distorted guitars frequently played with rapid
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tremolo_picking" title="Tremolo picking">
       tremolo picking
      </a>
      , a "dark" atmosphere
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genre.E2.80.94Death_Metal.2FBlack_Metal_158-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Genre.E2.80.94Death_Metal.2FBlack_Metal-158">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        158
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and intentionally
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lo-fi" title="Lo-fi">
       lo-fi
      </a>
      production, with ambient noise and background hiss.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162">
       <a href="#cite_note-162">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        162
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Satanic themes are common in black metal, though many bands take inspiration from ancient
      <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">
       paganism
      </a>
      , promoting a return to supposed pre-Christian values.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163">
       <a href="#cite_note-163">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        163
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Numerous black metal bands also "experiment with sounds from all possible forms of metal, folk, classical music, electronica and avant-garde".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-VS_157-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-VS-157">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        157
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Darkthrone" title="Darkthrone">
       Darkthrone
      </a>
      drummer
      <a href="/wiki/Fenriz" title="Fenriz">
       Fenriz
      </a>
      explains, "It had something to do with production, lyrics, the way they dressed and a commitment to making ugly, raw, grim stuff. There wasn't a generic sound."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campion_164-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Campion-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By 1990, Mayhem was regularly wearing
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corpsepaint" title="Corpsepaint">
       corpsepaint
      </a>
      ; many other black metal acts also adopted the look. Bathory inspired the
      <a href="/wiki/Viking_metal" title="Viking metal">
       Viking metal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Folk_metal" title="Folk metal">
       folk metal
      </a>
      movements and
      <a href="/wiki/Immortal_(band)" title="Immortal (band)">
       Immortal
      </a>
      brought blast beats to the fore. Some bands in the Scandinavian black metal scene became associated with considerable violence in the early 1990s,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165">
       <a href="#cite_note-165">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        165
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with Mayhem and Burzum linked to church burnings. Growing commercial hype around death metal generated a backlash; beginning in Norway, much of the Scandinavian metal underground shifted to support a black metal scene that resisted being co-opted by the commercial metal industry.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166">
       <a href="#cite_note-166">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        166
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to former
      <a href="/wiki/Gorgoroth" title="Gorgoroth">
       Gorgoroth
      </a>
      vocalist
      <a href="/wiki/Gaahl" title="Gaahl">
       Gaahl
      </a>
      , "Black Metal was never meant to reach an audience.... [We] had a common enemy which was, of course, Christianity, socialism and everything that democracy stands for."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campion_164-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Campion-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Mayhem_De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas.ogg" title="File:Mayhem De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas.ogg">
           "De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">

            </a>

            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">

            </a>

           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          The title track of
          <a href="/wiki/Mayhem_(band)" title="Mayhem (band)">
           Mayhem
          </a>
          's
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/De_Mysteriis_Dom_Sathanas" title="De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas">
            De Mysteriis Dom Sathanas
           </a>
          </i>
          (1994)
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      By 1992, black metal scenes had begun to emerge in areas outside Scandinavia, including Germany, France, and Poland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167">
       <a href="#cite_note-167">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        167
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The 1993 murder of Mayhem's
      <a href="/wiki/Euronymous" title="Euronymous">
       Euronymous
      </a>
      by Burzum's
      <a href="/wiki/Varg_Vikernes" title="Varg Vikernes">
       Varg Vikernes
      </a>
      provoked intensive media coverage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campion_164-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Campion-164">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        164
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Around 1996, when many in the scene felt the genre was stagnating,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168">
       <a href="#cite_note-168">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        168
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      several key bands, including Burzum and Finland's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beherit_(band)" title="Beherit (band)">
       Beherit
      </a>
      , moved toward an
      <a href="/wiki/Dark_ambient" title="Dark ambient">
       ambient
      </a>
      style, while
      <a href="/wiki/Symphonic_black_metal" title="Symphonic black metal">
       symphonic black metal
      </a>
      was explored by Sweden's
      <a href="/wiki/Tiamat_(band)" title="Tiamat (band)">
       Tiamat
      </a>
      and Switzerland's
      <a href="/wiki/Samael_(band)" title="Samael (band)">
       Samael
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169">
       <a href="#cite_note-169">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        169
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the late 1990s and early 2000s decade, Norway's
      <a href="/wiki/Dimmu_Borgir" title="Dimmu Borgir">
       Dimmu Borgir
      </a>
      brought black metal closer to the mainstream,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170">
       <a href="#cite_note-170">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        170
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as did
      <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Filth" title="Cradle of Filth">
       Cradle of Filth
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171">
       <a href="#cite_note-171">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        171
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">

         </div>

     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Power_metal">
       Power metal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Power metal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Power_metal" title="Power metal">
       power metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hammerfall_group.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1066" data-file-width="1582" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hammerfall_group.jpg/220px-Hammerfall_group.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hammerfall_group.jpg/330px-Hammerfall_group.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Hammerfall_group.jpg/440px-Hammerfall_group.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hammerfall_group.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Swedish power metal band
        <a href="/wiki/HammerFall" title="HammerFall">
         HammerFall
        </a>
        after a concert in
        <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
         Milan
        </a>
        , Italy, in 2005
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      During the late 1980s, the power metal scene came together largely in reaction to the harshness of death and black metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genre_-_Power_Metal_172-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Genre_-_Power_Metal-172">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        172
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Though a relatively underground style in North America, it enjoys wide popularity in Europe, Japan, and South America. Power metal focuses on upbeat, epic melodies and themes that "appeal to the listener's sense of valor and loveliness".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173">
       <a href="#cite_note-173">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        173
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The prototype for the sound was established in the mid-to-late 1980s by Germany's
      <a href="/wiki/Helloween" title="Helloween">
       Helloween
      </a>
      , which combined the power riffs, melodic approach, and high-pitched, "clean" singing style of bands like Judas Priest and Iron Maiden with thrash's speed and energy, "crystalliz[ing] the sonic ingredients of what is now known as power metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-174">
       <a href="#cite_note-174">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        174
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Traditional power metal bands like Sweden's
      <a href="/wiki/HammerFall" title="HammerFall">
       HammerFall
      </a>
      , England's
      <a href="/wiki/DragonForce" title="DragonForce">
       DragonForce
      </a>
      , and Florida's
      <a href="/wiki/Iced_Earth" title="Iced Earth">
       Iced Earth
      </a>
      have a sound clearly indebted to the classic NWOBHM style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175">
       <a href="#cite_note-175">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        175
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many power metal bands such as Florida's
      <a href="/wiki/Kamelot" title="Kamelot">
       Kamelot
      </a>
      , Finnish groups
      <a href="/wiki/Nightwish" title="Nightwish">
       Nightwish
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Stratovarius" title="Stratovarius">
       Stratovarius
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sonata_Arctica" title="Sonata Arctica">
       Sonata Arctica
      </a>
      , Italy's
      <a href="/wiki/Rhapsody_of_Fire" title="Rhapsody of Fire">
       Rhapsody of Fire
      </a>
      , and Russia's
      <a href="/wiki/Catharsis_(Russian_band)" title="Catharsis (Russian band)">
       Catharsis
      </a>
      feature a keyboard-based
      <a href="/wiki/Symphonic_metal#Symphonic_power_metal" title="Symphonic metal">
       "symphonic" sound
      </a>
      , sometimes employing orchestras and opera singers. Power metal has built a strong fanbase in Japan and South America, where bands like Brazil's
      <a href="/wiki/Angra_(band)" title="Angra (band)">
       Angra
      </a>
      and Argentina's
      <a href="/wiki/Rata_Blanca" title="Rata Blanca">
       Rata Blanca
      </a>
      are popular.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A-Z_of_Power_Metal_176-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-A-Z_of_Power_Metal-176">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        176
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Closely related to power metal is
      <a href="/wiki/Progressive_metal" title="Progressive metal">
       progressive metal
      </a>
      , which adopts the complex compositional approach of bands like
      <a href="/wiki/Rush_(band)" title="Rush (band)">
       Rush
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/King_Crimson" title="King Crimson">
       King Crimson
      </a>
      . This style emerged in the United States in the early and mid-1980s, with innovators such as
      <a href="/wiki/Queensr%C3%BFche" title="Queensrche">
       Queensrche
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fates_Warning" title="Fates Warning">
       Fates Warning
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Dream_Theater" title="Dream Theater">
       Dream Theater
      </a>
      . The mix of the progressive and power metal sounds is typified by New Jersey's
      <a href="/wiki/Symphony_X" title="Symphony X">
       Symphony X
      </a>
      , whose guitarist
      <a href="/wiki/Michael_Romeo" title="Michael Romeo">
       Michael Romeo
      </a>
      is among the most recognized of latter-day shredders.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Genre_-_Progressive_Metal_177-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Genre_-_Progressive_Metal-177">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        177
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Doom_metal">
       Doom metal
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Doom metal">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Doom_metal" title="Doom metal">
       doom metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <p>
      Emerging in the mid-1980s with such bands as California's
      <a href="/wiki/Saint_Vitus_(band)" title="Saint Vitus (band)">
       Saint Vitus
      </a>
      , Maryland's
      <a href="/wiki/The_Obsessed" title="The Obsessed">
       The Obsessed
      </a>
      , Chicago's
      <a href="/wiki/Trouble_(band)" title="Trouble (band)">
       Trouble
      </a>
      , and Sweden's
      <a href="/wiki/Candlemass" title="Candlemass">
       Candlemass
      </a>
      , the doom metal movement rejected other metal styles' emphasis on speed, slowing its music to a crawl. Doom metal traces its roots to the lyrical themes and musical approach of early Black Sabbath.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        178
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Melvins" title="Melvins">
       Melvins
      </a>
      have also been a significant influence on doom metal and a number of its subgenres.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        179
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Doom emphasizes melody, melancholy tempos, and a sepulchral mood relative to many other varieties of metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1_180-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYT1-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The 1991 release of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Forest_of_Equilibrium" title="Forest of Equilibrium">
        Forest of Equilibrium
       </a>
      </i>
      , the debut album by UK band
      <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_(band)" title="Cathedral (band)">
       Cathedral
      </a>
      , helped spark a new wave of doom metal. During the same period, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Death/doom" title="Death/doom">
       doom-death
      </a>
      fusion style of British bands
      <a href="/wiki/Paradise_Lost_(band)" title="Paradise Lost (band)">
       Paradise Lost
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/My_Dying_Bride" title="My Dying Bride">
       My Dying Bride
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Anathema_(band)" title="Anathema (band)">
       Anathema
      </a>
      gave rise to European gothic metal,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-181">
       <a href="#cite_note-181">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        181
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      with its signature dual-vocalist arrangements, exemplified by Norway's
      <a href="/wiki/Theatre_of_Tragedy" title="Theatre of Tragedy">
       Theatre of Tragedy
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Tristania_(band)" title="Tristania (band)">
       Tristania
      </a>
      . New York's
      <a href="/wiki/Type_O_Negative" title="Type O Negative">
       Type O Negative
      </a>
      introduced an American take on the style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-182">
       <a href="#cite_note-182">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        182
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Burning_Witch_Country_Doctor.ogg" title="File:Burning Witch Country Doctor.ogg">
           "Country Doctor"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>

            <source data-bandwidth="99253" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (99 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/b/bc/Burning_Witch_Country_Doctor.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
            </source>
            Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.
            <br/>
