             download the clip
            </a>
            or
            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
             download a player
            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          "Country Doctor" from
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Crippled_Lucifer" title="Crippled Lucifer">
            Crippled Lucifer
           </a>
          </i>
          (1998) by doom metal band
          <a href="/wiki/Burning_Witch" title="Burning Witch">
           Burning Witch
          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      In the United States,
      <a href="/wiki/Sludge_metal" title="Sludge metal">
       sludge metal
      </a>
      , mixing doom and hardcore, emerged in the late 1980s
      <a href="/wiki/Eyehategod" title="Eyehategod">
       Eyehategod
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crowbar_(US_band)" title="Crowbar (US band)">
       Crowbar
      </a>
      were leaders in a
      <a href="/wiki/Music_of_New_Orleans#Heavy_metal" title="Music of New Orleans">
       major Louisiana sludge scene
      </a>
      . Early in the next decade, California's
      <a href="/wiki/Kyuss" title="Kyuss">
       Kyuss
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sleep_(band)" title="Sleep (band)">
       Sleep
      </a>
      , inspired by the earlier doom metal bands, spearheaded the rise of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stoner_metal" title="Stoner metal">
       stoner metal
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        183
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while Seattle's
      <a href="/wiki/Earth_(American_band)" title="Earth (American band)">
       Earth
      </a>
      helped develop the
      <a href="/wiki/Drone_metal" title="Drone metal">
       drone metal
      </a>
      subgenre.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        184
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The late 1990s saw new bands form such as the Los Angelesbased
      <a href="/wiki/Goatsnake" title="Goatsnake">
       Goatsnake
      </a>
      , with a classic stoner/doom sound, and
      <a href="/wiki/Sunn_O)))" title="Sunn O)))">
       Sunn O)))
      </a>
      , which crosses lines between doom, drone, and
      <a href="/wiki/Dark_ambient" title="Dark ambient">
       dark ambient
      </a>
      metalthe
      <i>
       New York Times
      </i>
      has compared their sound to an "
      <a href="/wiki/Music_of_India" title="Music of India">
       Indian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Raga" title="Raga">
       raga
      </a>
      in the middle of an earthquake".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT1_180-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NYT1-180">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        180
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="New_fusions:_1990s_and_early_2000s">
       New fusions: 1990s and early 2000s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: New fusions: 1990s and early 2000s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote boilerplate seealso">
      For more details on this topic, see
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">
       alternative metal
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Nu_metal" title="Nu metal">
       nu metal
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Groove_metal" title="Groove metal">
       groove metal
      </a>
      .
     </div>
     <table class="mbox-small" style="border:1px solid #aaa;background-color:#f9f9f9;border:none; background:transparent; float:none; float:right">
      <tr>
       <td style="width:1px">
       </td>
       <td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="line-height:1.1em">
        <div class="haudio">
         <div style="padding:4px 0">
          <a href="/wiki/File:Panterawalk.ogg" title="File:Panterawalk.ogg">
           "Walk"
          </a>
         </div>
         <div>
          <div class="mediaContainer" style="position:relative;display:block;width:220px">
           <audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="30.031746031746" data-mwprovider="local" data-mwtitle="Panterawalk.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_9" poster="//en.wikipedia.org/static/1.26wmf13/resources/assets/file-type-icons/fileicon-ogg.png" preload="none" style="width:220px">
            <source data-bandwidth="113098" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (113 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8c/Panterawalk.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'>
            </source>
            Sorry, your browser either has JavaScript disabled or does not have any supported player.
            <br/>
            You can
            <a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/8c/Panterawalk.ogg">
             download the clip
            </a>
            or
            <a href="https://www.mediawiki.org/wiki/Special:MyLanguage/Extension:TimedMediaHandler/Client_download">
             download a player
            </a>
            to play the clip in your browser.
           </audio>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="description" style="padding:2px 0 0 0">
          "
          <a href="/wiki/Walk_(Pantera_song)" title="Walk (Pantera song)">
           Walk
          </a>
          " from
          <a href="/wiki/Pantera" title="Pantera">
           Pantera
          </a>
          's
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Display_of_Power" title="Vulgar Display of Power">
            Vulgar Display of Power
           </a>
          </i>
          (1992), exemplifying the groove metal style
         </div>
        </div>
        <hr/>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      The era of metal's mainstream dominance in North America came to an end in the early 1990s with the emergence of
      <a href="/wiki/Nirvana_(band)" title="Nirvana (band)">
       Nirvana
      </a>
      and other
      <a href="/wiki/Grunge" title="Grunge">
       grunge
      </a>
      bands, signaling the popular breakthrough of
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">
       alternative rock
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        185
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Grunge acts were influenced by the heavy metal sound, but rejected the excesses of the more popular metal bands, such as their "flashy and virtuosic solos" and "appearance-driven"
      <a href="/wiki/MTV" title="MTV">
       MTV
      </a>
      orientation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Covach_136-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Covach-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Glam metal fell out of favor due not only to the success of grunge,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        186
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but also because of the growing popularity of the more aggressive sound typified by Metallica and the post-thrash
      <a href="/wiki/Groove_metal" title="Groove metal">
       groove metal
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Pantera" title="Pantera">
       Pantera
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/White_Zombie_(band)" title="White Zombie (band)">
       White Zombie
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        187
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A few new, unambiguously metal bands had commercial success during the first half of the decadePantera's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Far_Beyond_Driven" title="Far Beyond Driven">
        Far Beyond Driven
       </a>
      </i>
      topped the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      chart in 1994but, "In the dull eyes of the mainstream, metal was dead".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        188
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some bands tried to adapt to the new musical landscape. Metallica revamped its image: the band members cut their hair and, in 1996, headlined the alternative musical festival
      <a href="/wiki/Lollapalooza" title="Lollapalooza">
       Lollapalooza
      </a>
      founded by
      <a href="/wiki/Jane%27s_Addiction" title="Jane's Addiction">
       Jane's Addiction
      </a>
      singer
      <a href="/wiki/Perry_Farrell" title="Perry Farrell">
       Perry Farrell
      </a>
      . While this prompted a backlash among some long-time fans,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189">
       <a href="#cite_note-189">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        189
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Metallica remained one of the most successful bands in the world into the new century.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        190
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Staley05.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="587" data-file-width="863" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Staley05.jpg/220px-Staley05.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Staley05.jpg/330px-Staley05.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Staley05.jpg/440px-Staley05.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Staley05.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Layne_Staley" title="Layne Staley">
         Layne Staley
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Chains" title="Alice in Chains">
         Alice in Chains
        </a>
        , one of the most popular acts identified with
        <a href="/wiki/Alternative_metal" title="Alternative metal">
         alternative metal
        </a>
        performing in 1992
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Like Jane's Addiction, many of the most popular early 1990s groups with roots in heavy metal fall under the umbrella term "alternative metal".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alternativemetal_191-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-alternativemetal-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Bands in Seattle's grunge scene such as
      <a href="/wiki/Soundgarden" title="Soundgarden">
       Soundgarden
      </a>
      , credited as making a "place for heavy metal in alternative rock",
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
       <a href="#cite_note-192">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        192
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alice_in_Chains" title="Alice in Chains">
       Alice in Chains
      </a>
      were at the center of the alternative metal movement. The label was applied to a wide spectrum of other acts that fused metal with different styles:
      <a href="/wiki/Faith_No_More" title="Faith No More">
       Faith No More
      </a>
      combined their alternative rock sound with punk,
      <a href="/wiki/Funk" title="Funk">
       funk
      </a>
      , metal, and
      <a href="/wiki/Hip_hop_music" title="Hip hop music">
       hip hop
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Primus_(band)" title="Primus (band)">
       Primus
      </a>
      joined elements of funk, punk,
      <a href="/wiki/Thrash_metal" title="Thrash metal">
       thrash metal
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Experimental_music" title="Experimental music">
       experimental music
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Tool_(band)" title="Tool (band)">
       Tool
      </a>
      mixed metal and
      <a href="/wiki/Progressive_rock" title="Progressive rock">
       progressive rock
      </a>
      ; bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Fear_Factory" title="Fear Factory">
       Fear Factory
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ministry_(band)" title="Ministry (band)">
       Ministry
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nine_Inch_Nails" title="Nine Inch Nails">
       Nine Inch Nails
      </a>
      began incorporating metal into their
      <a href="/wiki/Industrial_music" title="Industrial music">
       industrial sound
      </a>
      , and vice versa, respectively; and
      <a href="/wiki/Marilyn_Manson_(band)" title="Marilyn Manson (band)">
       Marilyn Manson
      </a>
      went down a similar route, while also employing shock effects of the sort popularized by Alice Cooper. Alternative metal artists, though they did not represent a cohesive scene, were united by their willingness to experiment with the metal genre and their rejection of glam metal aesthetics (with the stagecraft of Marilyn Manson and White Zombiealso identified with alt-metalsignificant, if partial, exceptions).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-alternativemetal_191-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-alternativemetal-191">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        191
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Alternative metal's mix of styles and sounds represented "the colorful results of metal opening up to face the outside world."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        193
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the mid- and late 1990s came a new wave of U.S. metal groups inspired by the alternative metal bands and their mix of genres.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        194
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Dubbed "nu metal", bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Slipknot_(band)" title="Slipknot (band)">
       Slipknot
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Linkin_Park" title="Linkin Park">
       Linkin Park
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Limp_Bizkit" title="Limp Bizkit">
       Limp Bizkit
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Papa_Roach" title="Papa Roach">
       Papa Roach
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/P.O.D." title="P.O.D.">
       P.O.D.
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">
       Korn
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Disturbed_(band)" title="Disturbed (band)">
       Disturbed
      </a>
      incorporated elements ranging from
      <a href="/wiki/Death_metal" title="Death metal">
       death metal
      </a>
      to hip hop, often including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DJ" title="DJ">
       DJs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Rapping" title="Rapping">
       rap
      </a>
      -style vocals. The mix demonstrated that "pancultural metal could pay off".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
       <a href="#cite_note-195">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        195
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nu metal gained mainstream success through heavy MTV rotation and Ozzy Osbourne's 1996 introduction of
      <a href="/wiki/Ozzfest" title="Ozzfest">
       Ozzfest
      </a>
      , which led the media to talk of a resurgence of heavy metal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        196
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1999,
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      noted that there were more than 500 specialty metal radio shows in the United States, nearly three times as many as ten years before.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197">
       <a href="#cite_note-197">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        197
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While nu metal was widely popular, traditional metal fans did not fully embrace the style.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198">
       <a href="#cite_note-198">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        198
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By early 2003, the movement's popularity was on the wane, though several nu metal acts such as
      <a href="/wiki/Korn" title="Korn">
       Korn
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Limp_Bizkit" title="Limp Bizkit">
       Limp Bizkit
      </a>
      retained substantial followings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        199
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_trends:_mid.E2.80.93late_2000s">
       Recent trends: midlate 2000s
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_metal_music&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Recent trends: midlate 2000s">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg">
        <img alt="A color photograph of two members of the group Children of Bodom standing on a stage with guitars, drums are visible in the background" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/200px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/300px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg/400px-Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Masters_of_Rock_2007_-_Children_of_Bodom_-_08.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Children_of_Bodom" title="Children of Bodom">
         Children of Bodom
        </a>
        , performing at the 2007
        <a href="/wiki/Masters_of_Rock_(festival)" title="Masters of Rock (festival)">
         Masters of Rock
        </a>
        festival
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Metal remained popular in the 2000s, particularly in continental Europe. By the new millennium Scandinavia had emerged as one of the areas producing innovative and successful bands, while Belgium, The Netherlands and especially Germany were the most significant markets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        200
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Established continental metal bands that placed multiple albums in the top 20 of the German charts between 2003 and 2008, including Finnish band
      <a href="/wiki/Children_of_Bodom" title="Children of Bodom">
       Children of Bodom
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        201
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Norwegian act Dimmu Borgir,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        202
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Germany's
      <a href="/wiki/Blind_Guardian" title="Blind Guardian">
       Blind Guardian
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
       <a href="#cite_note-203">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        203
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Sweden's HammerFall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        204
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Metalcore" title="Metalcore">
       Metalcore
      </a>
      , a hybrid of extreme metal and
      <a href="/wiki/Hardcore_punk" title="Hardcore punk">
       hardcore punk
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
       <a href="#cite_note-205">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        205
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      emerged as a commercial force in the mid-2000s decade. It is rooted in the
      <a href="/wiki/Crossover_thrash" title="Crossover thrash">
       crossover thrash
      </a>
      style developed two decades earlier by bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/Suicidal_Tendencies" title="Suicidal Tendencies">
       Suicidal Tendencies
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dirty_Rotten_Imbeciles" title="Dirty Rotten Imbeciles">
       Dirty Rotten Imbeciles
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Stormtroopers_of_Death" title="Stormtroopers of Death">
       Stormtroopers of Death
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
       <a href="#cite_note-206">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        206
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Through the 1990s, metalcore was mostly an underground phenomenon;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
       <a href="#cite_note-207">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        207
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      early bands include
      <a href="/wiki/Earth_Crisis" title="Earth Crisis">
       Earth Crisis
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
       <a href="#cite_note-208">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        208
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am_209-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-am-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ig_210-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ig-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Hogan%27s_Heroes_(band)" title="Hogan's Heroes (band)">
       Hogan's Heroes
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        211
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HistoryOfHardcore_212-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-HistoryOfHardcore-212">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        212
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Converge_(band)" title="Converge (band)">
       Converge
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-am_209-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-am-209">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        209
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Hatebreed" title="Hatebreed">
       Hatebreed
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ig_210-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ig-210">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        210
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rh_213-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-rh-213">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        213
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shai_Hulud_(band)" title="Shai Hulud (band)">
       Shai Hulud
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-killyour_214-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-killyour-214">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        214
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        215
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      By 2004, melodic metalcoreinfluenced as well by
      <a href="/wiki/Melodic_death_metal" title="Melodic death metal">
       melodic death metal
      </a>
      was popular enough that
      <a href="/wiki/Killswitch_Engage" title="Killswitch Engage">
       Killswitch Engage
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_End_of_Heartache" title="The End of Heartache">
        The End of Heartache
       </a>
      </i>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shadows_Fall" title="Shadows Fall">
       Shadows Fall
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_War_Within_(Shadows_Fall_album)" title="The War Within (Shadows Fall album)">
        The War Within
       </a>
      </i>
      debuted at numbers 21 and 20, respectively, on the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      album chart.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
       <a href="#cite_note-216">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        216
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="490" data-file-width="761" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG/200px-BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG/300px-BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG/400px-BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:BulletForMyValentine2006.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Bullet_for_My_Valentine" title="Bullet for My Valentine">
         Bullet for My Valentine
        </a>
        were one of the leading bands in the
        <a href="/wiki/Metalcore" title="Metalcore">
         metalcore
        </a>
        genre.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Welsh band
      <a href="/wiki/Bullet_for_My_Valentine" title="Bullet for My Valentine">
       Bullet for My Valentine
      </a>
      's third studio album
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Fever_(Bullet_for_My_Valentine_album)" title="Fever (Bullet for My Valentine album)">
        Fever
       </a>
      </i>
      debuted at position number 3 on
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Billboard_200" title="The Billboard 200">
       The Billboard 200
      </a>
      and number 1 on
      <a href="/wiki/Billboard_(magazine)" title="Billboard (magazine)">
       Billboard
      </a>
      's
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_music" title="Rock music">
       Rock
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_rock" title="Alternative rock">
       Alternative
      </a>
      charts, making it the band's most successful record to date. In recent years, metalcore bands have received prominent slots at Ozzfest and the
      <a href="/wiki/Download_Festival" title="Download Festival">
       Download Festival
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Lamb_of_God_(band)" title="Lamb of God (band)">
       Lamb of God
      </a>
      , a groove metal band, hit the
      <i>
       Billboard
      </i>
      top 10 in 2006 with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sacrament_(album)" title="Sacrament (album)">
        Sacrament
       </a>
      </i>
      . The success of these bands and others such as
      <a href="/wiki/Trivium_(band)" title="Trivium (band)">
       Trivium
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Avenged_Sevenfold" title="Avenged Sevenfold">
       Avenged Sevenfold
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        217
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which have released both metalcore and straight-ahead thrash albums. Bands like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Motionless_In_White" title="Motionless In White">
       Motionless In White
      </a>
      have experimented with Metalcore further by including Gothic Metal and Industrial Metal influences. Other notable experiments include
      <a href="/wiki/Asking_Alexandria" title="Asking Alexandria">
       Asking Alexandria
      </a>
      mixing Trance, this has led to an explosion of bands following this combination. One such band is
      <a href="/wiki/Silent_Descent" title="Silent Descent">
       Silent Descent
      </a>
      who mix Melodic Death Metal with trance. Evolving even further from metalcore comes
      <a href="/wiki/Mathcore" title="Mathcore">
       mathcore
      </a>
      , a more rhythmically complicated and progressive style brought to light by bands such as
      <a href="/wiki/The_Dillinger_Escape_Plan" title="The Dillinger Escape Plan">
       The Dillinger Escape Plan
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Converge_(band)" title="Converge (band)">
       Converge
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Protest_the_Hero" title="Protest the Hero">
       Protest the Hero
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        218
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mathcore's main defining quality is the use of odd time signatures, and has been described to possess rhythmic comparability to free Jazz.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        219
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Mastodon_(band)" title="Mastodon (band)">
       Mastodon
      </a>
      , which plays in a progressive/sludge style, has inspired claims of a metal revival in the United States, dubbed by some critics the "
      <a href="/wiki/New_Wave_of_American_Heavy_Metal" title="New Wave of American Heavy Metal">
       New Wave of American Heavy Metal
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
       <a href="#cite_note-220">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        220
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The term "retro-metal" has been used to describe bands such as Texas-based
      <a href="/wiki/The_Sword" title="The Sword">
       The Sword
      </a>
      , California's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High_on_Fire" title="High on Fire">
       High on Fire
      </a>
      , Sweden's
      <a href="/wiki/Witchcraft_(band)" title="Witchcraft (band)">
       Witchcraft
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMAgeofWinters_221-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-AMAgeofWinters-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Australia's
      <a href="/wiki/Wolfmother" title="Wolfmother">
       Wolfmother
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMAgeofWinters_221-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-AMAgeofWinters-221">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        221
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRS_222-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WRS-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Sword's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Age_of_Winters" title="Age of Winters">
        Age of Winters
       </a>
      </i>
      (2006) drew heavily on the work of Black Sabbath and Pentagram,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Begrand2006_223-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Begrand2006-223">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        223
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Witchcraft added elements of
      <a href="/wiki/Folk_rock" title="Folk rock">
       folk rock
      </a>
      and psychedelic rock,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        224
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Wolfmother's
      <a href="/wiki/Wolfmother_(album)" title="Wolfmother (album)">
       self-titled 2005 debut album
      </a>
      had "
      <a href="/wiki/Deep_Purple" title="Deep Purple">
       Deep Purple
      </a>
      -ish organs", "
      <a href="/wiki/Jimmy_Page" title="Jimmy Page">
       Jimmy Page
      </a>
      -worthy chordal
      <a href="/wiki/Ostinato" title="Ostinato">
       riffing
      </a>
      ", and lead singer
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Stockdale" title="Andrew Stockdale">
       Andrew Stockdale
      </a>
      howling "notes that
      <a href="/wiki/Robert_Plant" title="Robert Plant">
       Robert Plant
      </a>
      can't reach anymore".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WRS_222-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-WRS-222">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        222
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <p>
      More recently bands like Swedish-Danish sextet
      <a href="/wiki/Amaranthe" title="Amaranthe">
       Amaranthe
      </a>
      have fused Power Metal and Melodic Death Metal with what could be considered as the unorthodox sounds of Eurodance and Europop in a metal context.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Babymetal" title="Babymetal">
       Babymetal
      </a>
      , who have garnered a cult-following worldwide, mix Visual Kei, Metalcore-influences, dance and theatrical elements to both their music and live performances.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      On the Extreme Metal side, bands like
      <a href="/wiki/Sunn_O)))" title="Sunn O)))">
       Sunn O)))
      </a>
      have developed
      <a href="/wiki/Drone_metal" title="Drone metal">
       drone metal
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>

          <sup>

            <b>
             a

         <a href="#cite_ref-MH_28-1">
          <sup>

            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Dunn, Sam (2005).
         <a class="external text" href="http://www.metalhistory.com/" rel="nofollow">
          "Metal: A Headbanger's Journey"
         </a>
         . Warner Home Video (2006). Retrieved on March 19, 2007.

       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-29">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-29">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The first explicit prohibition of that interval seems to occur with the "development of
         <a href="/wiki/Guido_of_Arezzo" title="Guido of Arezzo">
          Guido of Arezzo
         </a>
         's
         <a href="/wiki/Hexachord" title="Hexachord">
          hexachordal
         </a>
         system which made B flat a
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diatonic" title="Diatonic">
          diatonic
         </a>
         note, namely as the 4th degree of the hexachordal on F. From then until the end of Renaissance the tritone, nicknamed the 'diabolus in musica', was regarded as an unstable interval and rejected as a consonance" (Sadie, Stanley [1980]. "Tritone", in
         <i>
          The New Grove Dictionary of Music and Musicians
         </i>
         , 1st ed. MacMillan, pp. 1545.
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0333231112">
          ISBN 0-333-23111-2
         </a>
         . See also Arnold, Denis [1983]. "Tritone", in
         <i>
          The New Oxford Companion to Music, Volume 1: A-J
         </i>
         . Oxford University Press.
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0193113163">
          ISBN 0-19-311316-3
         </a>
         ). During the
         <a href="/wiki/Baroque_music" title="Baroque music">
          Baroque
         </a>
         and
         <a href="/wiki/Classical_period_(music)" title="Classical period (music)">
          Classical
         </a>
         eras, the interval came to be accepted, though in a specific, controlled way. It is only during the
         <a href="/wiki/Romantic_music" title="Romantic music">
          Romantic era
         </a>
         and in
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/20th_century_classical_music" title="20th century classical music">
          modern classical music
         </a>
         that composers have used it freely, exploiting the evil connotations with which it is culturally associated.
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-rolling_memphis-73">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-rolling_memphis_73-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Miller, Jim (1980).
          <a class="external text" href="http://books.google.co.uk/books?id=3ZEUAQAAIAAJ" rel="nofollow">
           <i>
            The Rolling Stone illustrated history of rock &amp; roll
           </i>
          </a>
          .
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Rolling_Stone" title="Rolling Stone">
            Rolling Stone
           </a>
          </i>
          (New York).
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0-394-51322-3" title="Special:BookSources/0-394-51322-3">
           0-394-51322-3
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            5 July
           </span>
           2012
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           Black country bluesmen made raw, heavily amplified boogie records of their own, especially in Memphis, where guitarists like Joe Hill Louis, Willie Johnson (with the early Howlin' Wolf band) and Pat Hare (with Little Junior Parker) played driving rhythms and scorching, distorted solos that might be counted the distant ancestors of heavy metal.
          </q>

        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-101">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-101">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Though often identified now as "hard rock", the band's official debut album,
         <i>
          Mountain Climbing
         </i>
         (1970), placed 85th on the list of "Top 100 Metal Albums" compiled by
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Hit_Parader" title="Hit Parader">
           Hit Parader
          </a>
         </i>
         in 1989. In November,
         <a href="/wiki/Love_Sculpture" title="Love Sculpture">
          Love Sculpture
         </a>
         , with guitarist
         <a href="/wiki/Dave_Edmunds" title="Dave Edmunds">
          Dave Edmunds
         </a>
         , put out
         <i>
          Forms and Feelings
         </i>
         , featuring a pounding, aggressive version of
         <a href="/wiki/Khachaturian" title="Khachaturian">
          Khachaturian
         </a>
         's "
         <a href="/wiki/Sabre_Dance" title="Sabre Dance">
          Sabre Dance
         </a>
         ". Grand Funk Railroad's
         <i>
          Survival
         </i>
         (1971) placed 72nd (Walser [1993], p. 174).
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-208">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-208">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.ruleeverymoment.com/media/interviews/interview.php?id=43" rel="nofollow">
           "Shai Hulud, interview with Punknews.org - 05/28/08"
          </a>
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">

           </span>

          <q>
           As far as coining the term 'metalcore' or coining a sound, I don't think we did. There were bands before Shai Hulud started that my friends and I were referring to as 'metalcore'. Bands like Burn, Deadguy, Earth Crisis, even Integrity.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHeavy+metal+music&amp;rft.btitle=Shai+Hulud%2C+interview+with+Punknews.org+-+05%2F28%2F08&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.ruleeverymoment.com%2Fmedia%2Finterviews%2Finterview.php%3Fid%3D43&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">

        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-211">

       <li id="cite_note-HistoryOfHardcore-212">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-HistoryOfHardcore_212-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         HXC Revolution.
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.freewebs.com/mikeymisfit666" rel="nofollow">
           "History of HC"
          </a>
          . 2007-07-14
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2012-03-18
           </span>
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           Judge, Integrity and Hogan's Heroes were some of the earliest bands to bring this level of intensity to hardcorean amalgamation of deep, hoarse vocals (though rarely as deep or guttural as death metal); downtuned guitars and thrashy drum rhythms inspired by earlier hardcore bands; and slow staccato low-end breaks, known as breakdowns. Thrash metal and melodic death metal elements are also common in metalcore.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHeavy+metal+music&amp;rft.btitle=History+of+HC&amp;rft.date=2007-07-14&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.freewebs.com%2Fmikeymisfit666&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-rh-213">

       <li id="cite_note-killyour-214">

          <q>
           Shai Hulud, a name that is synonymous (in heavy music circles at least) with intelligent, provocative and most importantly unique metallic hardcore. The band's earliest release is widely credited with influencing an entire generation of musicians
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AHeavy+metal+music&amp;rft.btitle=Kill+Your+Stereo+-+Reviews%3A+Shai+Hulud+-+Misanthropy+Pure&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.killyourstereo.com%2Freviews%2F169%2Fshai-hulud-misanthropy-pure%2F&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
